system of malarial poison, and assist in the
formation of pure, ricli blood. These well-
known pills are comjmmuled of the essential
virtues of the best .vegetable cathartics, and
contain neither calomel nor any other deleteri-
ous drug. They may, therefore, be adminis-
tered in all cases where a purgative is needed,
without fear of ill effects. Being sugar-coated,
they are pleasant to take, and their medicinal
strength is preserved for an indefinite period

domestic medicine chest and traveller’s satchel.

Professor STINEWAY’S great success, which has
become so firmly established throughout Australasia,
is based upon the facts; - Ist. That every case treated,
even in its most remote effects, is specially studied,
thus starting aright. 2nd. The Medicines and Electric
Appliances used are exactly adapted to suit the case,'
thus promptly producing .results without overdosing
or injury.
I SEEK CASES WHERE ALL ELSE HAS FAILED.
Young and middle-aged men and others, who
suffer from Nervous and Physical Debility,
Exhausted Vitality, Premature Decline, Nc., can
recover the same degree of health, strength,
and energy experienced in former years, by
consulting (in rerson or by letter, which is free).
Professor CARL STINEWAY, who has restored
thousands of sufferers to vigorous manhood
after all other treatment had faded. It makes
no difference what you have taken, or who has
failed to cure you. Cases that I accept for treat-
ment are quickly and permanently cured,
DO ANY OF THE FOLLOWING SYMPTOMS
OR CLASS OF SYMPTOMS MEET YOUR
DISEASED CONDITION: —Are you a sufferer
from bad health in any of its many forms, consequent
on a lingering Nervous, Chronic, or Functional
Disease ? Do you feel nervous, debilitated, fretful,
timid, and lack the, pow nof will and action ? Are
you subject to loss of memory ; have spells of faint-
ing ; fullness of blood n the head; feel listless,
moping, unfit for business or pleasure, and subject to
fits of melancholy ? Are your kidneys, stomach,
urinary organs, liver, or blood in a disordered con-
dition ? Do you suffer from Rheumatism, Neuralgia,
or other aches and pains? Are you timid, nervous,
and forgetful, with your mind continually dwelling
on the subject? Have you lost confidence in yourself,
and energy for business pursuits? Are you subject
to restless nights, broken sleep, dreams, palpitation of
the heart, bashfulness, confusion of ideas, aversion
to society, dizziness in the head, dimness of sight,
pimples on the face and back, and other desponding
symptoms? If you are afflicted, why further neglect
your health or future happiness, when by a single
consultation (which costs nothing) you can be placed
on the way to health strength, and vigour?

TREATISE ON NERVOUS DISEASES, THEIR CAUSE,

rpilE directions contained in this little treatise will be
found of immense benefit to the sufferer ; and if these

Debility, Dizziness, Specks before the Byes, Pains in the

Constipation, Liver and Kidney Derangements, Atrophy,
Impotence, Impure Blood, Foul Taste in Mouth, Palpita-
tion of Heart. Wakefulness, Despondency, Excessive Ner-
vousness, Inability to Marry, Premature Decay, and other
symptoms experienced by nervous sufferers.

BHiL® THIS
Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoral ism.—Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only hut
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
Know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
•‘History repeals itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,'
the Capital of West Australia.

of five lots receive a free deed. Title (Torrens's) guaran-
teed to be indisputable.

SPECIAL MEDICAL NOTICE.
Dr. G. H. RAYMOND,
The Eminent
Botanic Physician, Specialist X Medical Writer,
Has commenced Practice in Sydney, after a residence of
four years in Queensland, twelve months in Western Aus-
tralia, nearly three years in South Australia, and eighteen
months in the City of Melbourne.
The latter place Mr. Raymond was compelled to abandon
in consequence of the climate proving detrimental to his
health.
During DR. RAYMOND’S practical experience of nine
years in Australia he has built up a reputation un-
approached by any other physician in chronic diseases in
the colonies. Dr. Raymond has also relieved and cured
patients who had tried to no avail the so-called leading
Inedical talent in the world. Proof of this will be seen in
his valuable and scientific publications; and Dr. Raymond
would call the attention of deaf people to his latest scien-
tific paper on the “Meritsand Demerits of Artificial Ear-

™ TT r, 10th December, 1888.
To 11. F. Kugklmann, Esq.
Dear Sir —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse everyday, masting away' until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last i began
to spit up blood, and what with the oolig’h and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of" bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became Very touch alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning* and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stritmv
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaming in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 4, 1890.
The “Reconstruction of Society.”
Exception was taken by Sir Henry Parkes.
in the N.S. Wales Legislative Assembly’s
debate relative to the Strike, to a phrase
which had been printed in the first manifesto
of the Labour Defence Committee. The
phrase was the “ reconstruction of society.”
Trades Unionists were invoked thus ; “ Stand
shoulder to shoulder in this movement; it
will give us heroic results, for the next step
in the immediate future will be the recon-
struction of society.” Sir Henry Parkes
challenged Mr. O’Sullivan, who had pre-
ceded him in the debate, to explain the
meaning of this passage. Mr. O'Sullivan’s
reply was a repudiation of it as the utterance
of “ 3 fanatic and a fool. ’ The Premier of
New South Wales proceeded to give his
interpretation. It proclaimed, to his mind,
“a war against all society.” There is here
a conspicuous want of logic on the part of
Sir Henr\ Parkes. Had the invocation
asked all Unionists to prepare for the des-
truction of society, that would certainly have
been equivalent to a declaration of war
against all society. But, being, as it is, an
invitation to reconstruct society, it is just
the contrary. If a person declared his inten-
tion to reconstruct his house, that could
assuredly not be charged against him as an
intimation that ho designed to destroy all
houses. It might signify either one of" two
intentions : namely, to repair an inconvenient
and dilapidated building, or to substitute by
a process of rebuilding a substantial structure
for one which had proved unserviceable or
was already a mere heap of ruins.
# * *
, There is, in fact, nothing in the expres-
sion that attracted the notice of the New
South W ales Premier, which is either novel
or startling. It is an old and familiar friend
a venerable acquaintance of now unchal-
lenged respectability—under a new name.
To “re-construct ” is simply to “re-form.”
All, therefore, that this paragraph of the
Labour Manifesto proclaimed was an invita-
tion to the people to continue a work, uni-
versally admitted to be good and advantageous
so far as it has been carried out—the work
of REFORM. There is this difference, in-
deed ; that whereas the initial efforts were
directed merely to remodel the Parliament
of Great Britain, the modern exertion is to
reconstruct society. Our forefathers, as it
were, concentrated their attention upon the
porter s lodge ; our own generation has
turned its attention to the main building.
The present revival of the Reform movement’
while presenting points of variation, is never-
theless characterised by identity in most
aspects. Of the earlier period of activity,
Harriet Martineau thus discourses
Up to the close of ISK3, the spirit of parliamentary
reform was seldom evoked in the British Parliament.
W hen the spirit was occasionally raised upon the presenta-
tion of some stray petition, it had no alarms for the most
timid, and very few consolations for the most ardent. It
was a good, quiet spirit in the cellarage—an “ old mole,”
that called out, in antiquated phrase, about the Magna
Charta and the Bill of Eights; and the House of
Commons listened as to some gabble which concerned it
not, and went on with its proper work of Whig and Tory
fence, conducted upon the most approved principles of
the first masters of the science. But the “ worthy
pioneer ” got above ground in 1816, and, when he was
fairly loosened to the open sky, he was not quite so tame,
and innocent, and stupid a spirit as his ordinary sup-
porters and his systematic revilers had been in the habit
of believing him to be.
* * %
In what manner this spirit of reform was
dealt with ; how it was lirst denounced as
factitious and the result of imposture ; and
how later it was exorcised—not exactly by
“bell, book and candle’’—but by bill, are
matters of history. The British people, like
Hamlet, permitting their purpose to be
diverted by mere temporary expedients, then
conceived that the spirit was laid. Their
agitation was intense, but transitory. To-day
the apparition re-appears. “Do notfoi’get,”
says the spirit of Reform, “this visitation is
but to whet thy almost blunted purpose.”
Che British people—the Hamlet of this
drama of the nineteenth century—resume
the interrupted thread of their determina-
tion. -Sow, at last, they will obey the in-
junction and prompting of the spirit, and
cany out their intention to its full accom-
plishment, rejecting with scorn the sugges-
tion of interested and compromised parties
that the whole thing is a delusion. The
guilty Queen, it will be remembered, insists
that
This is the very coinage of your brain ;
This bodiless creation ecstasy is very cunning in.
In like manner, the Classes and their organs
would fain persuade the reformers or re-
constructors of to-day, that their ideals are
mere figments of a diseased imagination,
that there are no wrongs to be righted, no
usurpations to be swept away. But the
British people rejoin :
It ia not madness
That I have uttered: bring me to the test,
And I the matter will reward, which madness
Would gambol from. . . .
Lay not that flattering unction to your soul
That not your madness but my maclness speaks*
It will but skin and film the ulcerous place,
hile rank corruption, mining all within, infects unseen.
* * *
That is to say, the revived party of Reform
retort to those who allege that they are but
dreamers and visionaries, that they are
prepared to sustain their case with argu-
ments as rational, as logical, and as lucid, as
any that were ever advanced on behalf of
any cause however solidly established and
widely approved. They are enabled, in
fact, to point to a mass of literature of the
highest order, in which their views and their
philosophy are expounded and advocated.
Those claims which from the year 1816 for-
ward they urged, have, after undergoing the
ordeals of ridicule, of denunciation, and of
reluctant examination, at length gained
respectful, even though hostile, investiga-
tion, and finally secured adoption and the
stamp of orthodoxy. They now advance a
further instalment, and submit with cheer-
fulness to the same preliminary treatment,
confident that the result will be the same as
in their earlier attempt. There is nothing
to disconcert the reformers or reconstructors
of to-day in the circumstance that their con-
ceptions are derided, that their leaders
are assailed with detraction, or that some
of their adherents offend the delicate
susceptibilities of conservatism. They
remember that when the cause of Reform
was lirst mooted even so liberal a man
as Brougham, who later was one of the
most valiant of its champions, described it,
in the House of Commons, as “ opposed by
some, deserted by others, and espoused by
persons whose conduct excited no small
degree of disgust out of doors.”
* * *
It is strange, indeed, that any man pretend-
ing to statesmanlike breadth of thought
should have been staggered by evidence that
Trades-b nionism recognises its own ten-
dency and frankly proclaims that its mission
is to effect a reconstruction of Society. A
very slight study of the past effects of
Trades-Unionism, and a very superficial
examination of its influence should have
shown that its results have been confined to
that very reconstruction, although the
changes wrought have been, up to the pre-
sent stage, only partial. Since the year
1825, when the penal laws against trades-
combinations to raise wages were relaxed,
the effects of Trades-Unions have been,
beyond question, to bring about a partial
reconstruction of society. Large sections of
workers—chiefly in these departments of
industry which demand a degree of skill and
handicraft only to be achieved by long
practice—have been emancipated from a
condition of almost absolute serfdom and
have gained power to strike bargains for the
sale of their labour. Men, who were chattels,
have, by combination, been enabled to look
their former owners straight in the face, and
not merely to refuse to be starved, but to
stipulate for something (no matter how little)
more than a mere sufficiency to keep the
breath of life in them, and even—to the
horror and indignation of their masters—
to tell them on what conditions work shall
be oondueted if he desires their aid. This is
an attitude which still fills the emplover
class with wrathful astonishment. It is
monstrous, he declares, it is outrageous and
ridiculous that his servants should pretend
to dictate to him how he shall carry on and
conduct “ his own business.” It has not yet
dawned upon men of this class that what he
calls his business is also very particularly the
business of every man—the scores or
hundreds who work' at it. The profits
indeed are his, but the business is that of
each of his “ hands,” and it is ©r may be of
momentous importance to each and all of
them that it be conducted on certain broadly
defined lines, insofar as regards their in-
terests.
* # *
It must be admitted that there has been a
very material reconstruction of society by
the operation of Trades-Unions already.
And there are unmistakeable signs that the
manifesto which has so shocked the venerable
personage at the head of the New South
Wales Ministry, quite accurately defines the
course of events when it predicts an ex-
tension of that operation. The benefits of
Trades-Unionism are at length commencing
to be participated in by other classes of
workers besides the order of skilled
mechanics, miners, and factory-hands. The
mere labourer, who has nought to sell but
his physical strength, has discovered the
efficiency of combination, and on the
first effort that of the London dock-
labourers—has scored a sensational win.
This event has had vast influence.
Its results are, as yet, mainly in the
womb of the future, and can only be
appreciated by such persons as are skilled
in political and economic obstetrics. But
this much is plain to every sensible man’s
observation and consciousness, that the
victory won by the London dock-labourers
has aroused an emotion of hope and a desire
- probably also an intention of emulation,
in the minds of millions of mere labourers
all over the civilised world. Such an emotion,
so far-reaching, has never yet taken a hold
of the minds of so great a multitude without
producing effects of corresponding magni-
tude. The effects in this instance will,
beyond doubt, take the direction of a
further instalment of the 44 re-construction
society.”
* * *
Nevertheless, it is necessary to recognise
that there are distinct limits to the adequacy
of Irades-unionism as a mechanism for the
amelioration in the condition of the working
class. The direction in which its develop-
ments are now plainly tending is towards
the inclusion and organisation of all wage-
earning people into unions. And such
unions it is intended to federate in one
common interest. The first of these opera-
tions bears within it the seeds of destruction.
I nder existing conditions there is not con-
stant employment available at decent re-
muneration for all the labour which is in
the market. Even were unions to pursue
the traditional policy of the old-unionism and
restrict their membership, thus constituting
what has been aptly termed Labour-aristo-
cracy, the competition of excluded labourers
would break the back-bone of the unions,
by enabling employers to defy their claims
and to replace their members by the too
redundant crowd of outsiders. If, on the
contrary, the Unions held their barriers
open to every brother aspirant for work, the
excess of Unionists would necessarily have
the same effect, and wages would fall. The
upward move of wages consequent on the
power of Unions would assuredly not tend to
increase consumption of the commodities
produced for exchange outside the com-
munity in which they were produced. Em-
ployers cannot make profitable work without
limit. Even Co-operation—from which so
much is expected—would not by any means
realise what is claimed for it. If, for example,
the existing coastal maritime carrying trade
were shifted from the present competing
employer-companies into the hands of the
State, there would not be more hands needed
There would be fewer. Under present
arrangements, more steamers have been run
than there has been full employment for.
Of these, a proportion are run to waste, as
it were. And under a proper communal
system, all waste would be eschewed. There-
fore, the crews would be reduced in numbers.
• • *
There is no escaping the conclusion that
employers are exceedingly blind and foolish.
They are doomed to be crushed between the
upper mill-stone of rent and the lower mill-
stone of wages. All along the Australian
coast, for years past, steamship-owners have
been doing carrying business too cheaply.
Somebody has benefited thereby. Not the
seamen, and certainly not the officers. The
former of these classes has extorted some
approach to fair remuneration out of the
groaning employers by sheer force of com-
bination. The latter, uncombined, could
not get a concession. But who, then, has
leaped the advantage ? The land-owners.
Ihe cheaper the carriage to market, the
larger the nett-profits from cultivation. If
the cultivator were himself the owner of the
land, the larger the return into his own
pocket—not as cultivator, indeed, but as
landlord. If he were but a tenant, then the
landlord would, of course, in due time
raise his rent by just the amount saved him
by over-cheapness of carriage to market.
Ihus, it will be seen that Trades-Unionism
is but an expedient and without fundamental
security. _ The causes of human inequality
of conditions lie deeper and beyond the re«"h
of Unionism.
* * *
But although merely an instrument,
Irades-Umonism has its potencies. It can
exert a vast influence by giving sway to its
political side. At the present juncture the
duty of its leaders is so plain that no man can
mistake. It is to prepare to take possession
of the Legislature. It is gratifying to observe
from published statements that the Unionist
leaders have grasped the essentialness
of this action. There is still one
pitfall, however, in the path, which
it will require all their sagacity, all th '
vigilance, and no inconsiderable
self-denial and of their influence to eual l
them to escape. That is the natural disno •
tion to put forward actual members of th
own organisations as Labour candidate' 1 '
This process will be successfully resorted t
we doubt not, in course of time. But -° :
yet, nothing but failure and injury can h*
anticipated if it be resorted to. I n eve D ®
instance in which recourse has been had t?
it, the result has been calamitous. r pj
Labour candidates have not been
and the successful competitors have obtain ? j
their seats free from any pledges or oblhr!
tions to the labour voters. The real!
sagacious plan is that adopted by the teetotV
bodies. _ Everything is disregarded save th,!
one object. Candidates of all parties are
called upon to declare their sentiments and
shufflers are inexorably treated as rnorHl
foes. 1
* * *
This is precisely the expedient the Labour
party should adopt. They should turn a
deaf ear to every call to arms, save those
sounded by their own trumpets. Between
contending parties they should stand aloof
in imposing array, potent to give the victory
to this or to that combatant—providing
their own stipulations be conceded. PoJi"
ticians are a weak-backed and temporising
race. There are at least 100,000 men synn
pathetic with Unionism and entitled to a
vote, in New South Wales alone. Such a
body, properly organised and handled
should be able to turn, to their own advan-
tage, every election in the coming series
which may be contested between Protec-
tionist and Freetrader, or any other rival
cult. To the true adherent of the enfran-
chisement of Labour, Protection and Free-
trade, the Orange and the Green, the Pub-
lican and the Water-jugger, should be
equally indifferent. The vote of Labour
should be given to the candidate who,
being worthy of trust at all, declares him-
self most definitely in favour of the principles
of the Labour propaganda. And that
should be short, clear, and decisive. Every
elected member should be carefully watched
as to his votes and expressions in Parlia-
ment. Humbugs and traitors should meet
with unflinching vengeance the next time
they have to face their constituents. By
those means, and those only, will the wage-
earners—the masses—of Australia be enabfed
to secure their real proportion of influence
on its public affairs, and thus only will it
ever be possible here to effect that recon-
struction of Society which must be accom-
plished before the producer of wealth can
hope to share in its enjoyments.
£12 4s, 9d.
Mu. Jacob Goldstein, of the Melbourne
Charity Organisation Society, was invited the
other day to give evidence before the Charities
Commission of Victoria, and in the course of
his testimony lie cast a lurid light on the ways
of modern benevolence. During a period of 12
months the organised benefactors who hire Mr.
Goldstein have expended about £460 in the
prosecution of their noble purpose, and of this
sum—all of which was raised by public subscrip-
tion—£l2 4s. 9d. went to assist the poor and
the suffering, and to alleviate in some measure
the aggregate of human misery; and the
balance was used for salaries, expenses, and
sundries. A good deal of the money extracted
from a sympathetic nation appears to have
gone to pay the energetic “ enquiry-officer ”
a species of bowelless committee of one, whose
business it is to root out the morals, religious
convictions, genealogy, height, length, breadth
and thickness of every applicant for assistance,
so that no one save an apostle may get a cent,
out of the funds ; and the ghastly vigour with
which this gentleman shunted the poor who
weren’t orthodox, and the hungry whose heads
were not surmounted by a halo, stands out
conspicuously in every separate copper of that
£l2 4s. 9d. The amount which this capable
official must have saved by his investigations is
evidently tremendous, and but for his un-
exampled vigour it would have been difficult to
reserve enough to pay expenses. Still it is
painful to reflect that with a little more super-
human energy the outlay of that £l2 4s. 9d.
might have been avoided, and when once this
unjustifiable leakage was stopped the Charity
Organisation Society would be able to devote
itself exclusively to the lofty mission of paying
salaries, rent, gas, and other charges, and
meanwhile letting the poor go to the tomb.
If an enquiry - officer only enquires hard
enough he can find good and sufficient
proof that nobody on earth is moral enough
to deserve the gift of a loaf or a bed;
his mission is to squash the applicant for relief,
and throw him out into the street, and if a £250
officer is not vigorous enough to overpower and
eject £l2 4s. 9d. worth of tattered mendicant it
is time that a stronger man took his place. The
purpose of the organised benefactor is not to
relieve the famished outcast—that is an ex-
ploded superstition based on the injudicious
lavishness of an unorganised Redeemer, who
went about recklessly feeding the poor in
Palestine, and died at last without ever pub-
lishing a balance-sheet, or even hiring a secre-
tary ; his business is first of all to ask the out-
cast what possible right he has to be famished,
and having done this he tells the applicant to
call round the week after next. And in the
interval thus afforded he spends £5 in inquiries
and travelling expenses, and having duly in-
vestigated the case he is generally in a position
to tell the hungry wreck to depart into outer
darkness, because he was drunk in the year
1853, or was arrested for assault and battery a
generation ago. The only possible improve-
ment on this system would be to fire the pauper
over the staircase on his first appearance, and
devote the time which is now wasted in listen-
ing to his tale of woe to the collection of more
subscriptions ; and then the travelling expenses


eouKl be saved, and the amount of this economy
demoted to raising the salary of the toilworn
secretary, and the inquiry officer could have the
sum which is now wasted in riotous postage-
stamps, and the rent could bo divided among
the Board, and the gas-money and sundries
might be scrambled for by anybody who wanted
fche ° m - *
'Phe charity organisation system, however, is
eß ly in its infancy, and the injudicious scat-
toration of £l2 4s. 9d. arose most probably from
causes which are not likely to recur. The great
movement is hampered a good deal by the action
of various ill-judged philanthropists who relieve
the homeless wanderer instead of throwing him
out, and who have not yet realised the lofty
fcruth that benevolence consists in collecting
subscriptions, and bidding the sufferer come
and be relieved, and then, when he does come,
chasing him into the hills with a club. Also
there is a lack of system about many of these
individuals, and they even feed a starving
wretch right off, instead of spending two or
three weeks in making inquiries, while he dies
at night in a damp spot behind a fence. Mr.
Goldstein therefore urges that something should
be done to awaken the hardened and irregular
benefactor, and show him the evil of a system
which has no “ inquiry officer” or travelling
expenses in it, and which is loose on the subject
of gas and balance-sheets. He appeals to the
Commission to lend a hand in bouncing out the
irregular Samaritan, and in suppressing the
good shepherd whose money goes to the hungry
instead of to the secretary, and he has various
other complaints as well.
Unfortunately, charitable societies overlapped each
Other in some places. This was the case at Brunswick,
where there were two ladies’ benevolent societies, who
were working in opposition to one another. The same
fact was noticeable in Collingwood. He also objected to
the admission of the purely casually destitute into some
of the Melbourne charitable institutions. They had much
better he taken care of in the gaols. The institutions to
which he referred were the casual wards of the Immi-
grants’ Home, the shelter in Dr. Sinoleton’s Home, and
the casual ward in the St. Vincent de Paul Home. The
great defect of these institutions was that no records
were kept, and that relief was granted on application.
The voice of the £l2 4s. 9d. cries aloud in this
utterance. For if the “ casually-destitute ” are
shoved into gaol as the due reward of their
offence, then the only thing that can meet the
ease of the permanently destitute is to hang
them or throw them over a precipice, and when
the people who are in need of charity are thus
disposed of the Charity Organisation Society
can collect the money of the public with a glad
heart and organise itself all day long and pay
salaries the rest of the time. And yet there is
base ingratitude in the secretary’s suggestion,
for it is the existence of the unfed, unemployed
citizen, who is awarded the humble sum with
the odd coppers at the end of it, that insures
him his copious income and keeps the whole
show going. A society which collects money
for the aged pauper and spends over nine-
tenths of the takings upon itself, and then
blasts the starving outcast who forms its own
stock-in-trade, and wants him put in gaol on
top of his other miseries, is a thing without
bowels or compassion, or even ordinary horse-
sense of the plainest kind.
* * *
Still there is one redeeming feature in this
picture of latter-day philanthropy. The Society
actually did bestow upon the poor the aforesaid
sum of £l2 4s. 9d. Also, there is no reason to
believe that it plundered any applicant of his
clothes, and sold them to help to pay the salary
of the inquiry officer, or to defray the gas-bill.
If it successfully absorbed in “ expenses ” over-
-90 per cent, of the money subscribed for
the tattered mendicant, and cursed other phil-
anthropists for doing the same, it refrained at
least from capturing the mendicant, and boiling
him down, and trading off the products. The
Christian who giveth to the poor through the
medium of the Charity Organisation Society
sifts his money through a ladder, but it is cheer-
ing to reflect that the amount that adheres to
the steps ultimately reaches its destination—
the sum which falls through being duly collared
to pay the secretary. Evidently the steps are
greased, and the ladder planted at an acute
slope, as a guarantee against too much adhe-
sion, but that is a mere detail. And if, as
seems more than probable, the joint-stock bene-
factors may be inspired at an early date to erect
the three-ball sign above their door, and put the
secretary’s honoured name upon the tickets,
then the industry will stand on a sound basis,
and assume its proper attitude before the world.
And, meanwhile, the benevolent citizen who
has compassion on the widow and the fatherless
is earnestly requested to send round his cheque,
and the secretary will go out on his behalf and
call on the widow and the fatherless in a cab,
which will be charged to the cash account, and
will point out that they ought to he in gaol,
and will tell them of the sinfulness of granting
relief either “ on application ’ or any other way,
and will depart again with the joyous reflection
that his salary is going on all the time.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Divorce by Consent.
Judge Hood, of Victoria, is again tangled up
in the Divorce Act. The other day he refused
an application by a woman whose husband
abandoned her twelve years ago, on the ground
that she had not exerted herself to recover his
affections and win him back ; and unless there
was proof that she had done so, it was feared
that a pernicious system of divorce by collusion
or mutual consent might be inaugurated. It
would be interesting to know why divorces
should not be granted by mutual consent. As
a rule, when two people have contracted a re-
liable hatred for each other, they are bound to
separate, and a law which requires that a woman
should be severely kicked, or starved and neg-
lected for three years, before she can obtain the
release which she desires, and which her husband
is perfectly willing to grant, is idiotic beyond
expression. If husband and wife are both quite
tired of being together, that is as good a reason
as could be imagined why they should be parted,
and when the meddlesome State forcibly keeps
them together nobody is any the better for its
interference, and its grandmotherly care pays
no dividends. Its nominal object is to secure
the happiness of all parties concerned ; but as
the State cares not one solitary rap for any-
body’s happiness, this explanation is absurdly
thin. Divorce by mutual consent is declared to
have proved itself an unmixed evil wherever it
has been tried ; but then it never has been tried
in any civilised country. Divorce by mutual
agreement, suppplemented by hard public
lying, is no doubt sufficiently common ; but
then lying, whether public or private, is
a failure under all circumstances, and yet, even
in the most advanced communities, it is an
absolutely essential element when two people
are weary of each other’s society, and wish to
separate before a worse thing befalls them. The
sanctity of unhappy marriages is merely the
tail-end of an old, exploded superstition, and
the law has no more right to decree that any
human being shall be miserable, than it has to
declare that lie shall be damp and afflicted with
corns. The parsonical objection, “Whom (Tod
hath joined let no man put asunder,” is effete
and out of date, for God is neither the solemn
sin-shifter who gabbles over the marriage-
service in an empty, hollow-sounding church,
nor the dreary registrar who shuffles through
the civil formula in a dusty office, and where
man takes the fees he must also assume the
responsibility. And if divorce by mutual con-
sent is a thing of ghastly iniquity, too awful to
lie seriously considered in an alleged Christian
land, we would be glad if .fudge Hood, or any
other Judge, would suggest a logical substitute.
The natural antithesis of this sinful device
would be divorce without the consent of either
party, and Mr. Hood’s opinions upon that ought
to be worth hearing.
The Coming Man.
Governor Jersey is still some distance off, but
already between 1900 and 2000 paragraphs have
been published in different parts of Australia
setting forth the blessings and advantages of
the new Deputy-Gracious. Asa consequence,
Jersey is getting dreadfully monotonous before
ho has even started. Moreover, all the descrip-
tions of him are as commonplace as a heap of
mud, and the enthusiasm is dreadfully heavy
and tough in consequence. The new Governor
is as hopelessly devoid of thrill as a bun in a
shop-window, and even his most devoted wor-
shippers can find nothing to say about him that
is more novel or suprising than a pump would
be. The sample paragraph generally goes like
this
Lord Jersey is a pleasant and fluent speaker, a man of
wealth and liberality, and well versed in affairs. In
Oxfordshire, of which county he is Lord-Lieutenant, he
enjoys great popularity, and has always taken the lead in
every useful and philanthropic movement. It is a great
thing to find that noblemen of wealth and ability, with a
political career before them at home, are still willing to
accept responsible and arduous posts in the colonies.
All this simply means that Governor Jersey is
an inexpensive individual of the ordinary size
who looks very much as usual. He showed no
particular signs of genius when he was young,
and has shown none since. He was born in the
same way as anybody else, and he grew up on
his own responsibility. Also he never did any-
thing worth printing, and he has a look in his
eye which might signify latent genius, or might
merely mean that he was wondering where his
dog had got to, and he has two legs and never
did any serious harm. All the eloquence of
several hundred papers amounts to that and
nothing more. And consequently The Bulletin
would be glad to know what all the row is
about.
The “Free Labourer.”
The “free labourer so called because he is
liable to be bossed within an inch of his life by
his master without any prospect or possibility
of redress—promises to become a A r ery serious
element in Australian society. He is a non-
descript hanger-on of the great army of industry
—an individual who (like the Institute of
Marine Engineers) reaps where he has not sown
and curses the individual who sowed for him—
and his own future depends entirely in the suc-
cess of the Trades-Unionism which he denounces
with so much unnecessary vehemence. Conse-
quently his position is an anomalous one, for if
Unionism survives he will lose his temporary
importance, and if Unionism dies he will lose
his importance just the same, and along with it
he will lose everything else that makes life
worth having. Whatever Labour has gained
within the last century it has gained by com-
bination. The workman of years ago was a
serf of the meanest order. He toiled from 12
to 18 hours daily in a noisome factory, and
along with him his wife and his children
laboured in the same stifling atmosphere of dust
and fluff and disease, for no single worker, how-
ever industrious, could earn more than enough
for his own subsistence. The unceasing round
of grinding Labour began in infancy, and from
the age of five years the children of the people
dragged in their weary, stunted existence and
spent their joyless lives pent up between the
high brick walls of huge workshops, where, in
many cases they died as well as lived, worn out
by the premature old age which came upon them
when their childhood was only dawning. But
gradually, even amid this hideous slavery,
the workman learned the advantages to be
gained by combination, and after long years
of agitation and strife he achieved the
right to be a little more of a human
being and a little less of a mere animal than
heretofore. Philanthropy and Christianity did
nothing for him ; what liberty he acquired was
gained by the brute force of numbers and
material pressure, and it has since been retained
by the same influences. It was Unionism in the
past which earned those privileges which make
it worth the “ free labourer’s ” while to be alive
and without which he would be to-day what his
great-grandfather was—something a shade lower
than a dog and of less account than a horse ;
and it is the Unionism of the present which
alone saves him from reverting to the hopeless
slavery and degradation of his ancestors. The
Unionist fights single-handed the endless battle
by which alone Labour preserves its liberty,
and in sacrificing himself to raise the status of
Labour he necessarily assists his enemy, the
blackleg, as well as himself; and the latter
reviles Tiim for doing it, and grabs the plunder.
And yet, if he succeeds in his effort to destroy
Unionism he only insures his own destruction
Monopoly is no less grasping and unscrupulous
to-day than it was years ago when that eminent
philanthropist and public benefactor, Mr.
Bright, declared himself ready to die in the
last ditch to maintain the old system under
which women and children died of exhaust ion
and hunger and ill-usage in the factories where
he himself made his sanctified pile : and Chris-
tianity is no more Christian than it was then.
The employer in England or Australia is moder-
ately humane—by Act of Parliament; but the
employer of the same race in Bombay, where that
Act of Parliament is inoperative, lias revived
all the old horrors of slavery in its worst form.
The monopolist of Australia is of the same
brotherhood as the monopolist of London, who
has his cash invested in the Zanzibar slave-
trade, and who farms the convict-worked mines
of Siberia. The love of money is incurable,
ineradicable, and unchangeable, and almost the
only honest name to be found in history is that
of Judas, who handed back the thirty pieces of
silver and then hanged himself. Trades-
Unionism is as essential for the defence of
Labour now as it was in the reign of George
the Imbecile and his worst predecessors, and
the so-called “ free-labourer,” who seeks to
undermine it for his own passing advantage, is
in the position of a rescued man who endeavours
to throw his rescuer overboard on the ground
that the boat which has saved him Cannot afford
room for two. And if he succeeds in his effort
his reward is already awaiting both him and his
descendants to the tenth generation, and his
memory w ill be cursed by posterity along with
those of Gehazi and Benedict Arnold and
many another gentleman of the same order.
The G-rovel of the Drapery Trade.
Dear Bulletin, —WheneverCapitaland Labour
are at war, and commotion is in the air, and the
parochial sycophant is in motion to uphold the
cause of “ law and order,” the Australian draper
comes to the front. Why grovel and drapery
should invariably go together is a problem
which can only be solved by deep scientific
research, but experience has proved them to be
inseparable elements. The lady-like he-female
with the yardstick represents the one branch of
industry that never was known to strike a blow r
in its own defence. It is underpaid and over-
worked and trodden upon, and it serves as a
humble target for bad language of- all sorts and
descriptions, and yet it is the only worm on
record which never turns. It turns fiat, and
that is all. When employers and employed are
at variance, and a great effort is being made to
elevate the status of Labour, the gilded duke
who flies round in the ribbon department always
casts in the weight of his little soul with the
cause of property. He has no property himself
and no prospect of any, but the very name of
Labour suggests perspiration and dust and
brawny vulgarity, and all these influences are
antagonistic to the correct hang of his pants and
the accurate adjustment of his coat-tail, and
the attitude of his collar. Therefore the draper
fills the ranks of the special constables, and
humps bales and boxes and unclean bags on the
wharf at a crisis, and breaks his back gene-
rally to prop up the cause of monopoly.
He does these things partly because it
is respectable to manifest his antagonism to
that low and offensive thing, the ordinary
every-day toiler, and partly because he would
be sacked if he refused to do them. He
exhibits no resentment at this state of things
for he is a being apart; he belongs to Labour,
but he refuses to recognise the fact lest he
should burst his tie in doing it, and he affects to
be an essential part of Capital and is kicked in
consequence. He is neither man nor woman
nor any other recognised institution—only a
draper. He is the backbone of Australian
“ loyalty,” the prop and stay of the Imperial
Federation movement, and the principal sup-
port of the Primrose organisation and the Social
Purity Society ; also, he hollers loud and long
whenever the name of his beloved Queen comes
to the front. He is a social mystery, for
nobody knows what becomes of him when he
gets old, and nobody—so far as we can learn —
ever saw a dead draper or attended the funeral
of one. Probably he is sacked when he ceases
to be young and beautiful, and then he either
bursts or evaporates and fades away. No
historian has recorded that a draper ever rose
to eminence or did anything for the good of his
country. One of the great names on the scroll
of Fame is that of the negro flunkey who
asserted the independence of San Domingo, but
the weary little dude in the glove and sock
department never did anything but oil his hair
and look elegant. And yet the poor, servile,
stunted he-milliner is a serious obstruction to
human progress. His grovel is so far-reaching
and complete, and his subserviency so
illimitable, that he is a public evil as well as a
nuisance. He is incapable of elevating himself
on account of his collar, and he is filled with an
arrogant longing to suppress everybody else
whose collar isn’t up to the mark. He shudders
when he hears that an individual in a woollen
shirt and large boots has ventured to assert
himself, and his soul revolts—at 30s. a week—
when he conies in contact with a low, coarse
person who drives a cart and hasn’t any eye-
glass. And, worst of all, he is utterly devoid
of sympathy with any democratic sentiment or
any progressive idea, because, unhappily, he
knows no better, being only a draper.— Titus
Salt.
Tlxe Deification of Dow.
Dear Bulletin, —As an admirer of unblushing
cheek, I would like to put John Lamont Dow,
the Victorian Minister of Lands, upon a
pedestal. A few years back, Dow invested £2
in the Premier Permanent Building Society,
now (probably permanently) defunct. Somehow
or other he got jammed in among other notable
persons and was elected a Director. He sat as
a Director, drew Directors’ fees, signed cheques
for many thousands of pounds of other people’s
money—all the time not having the necessary
legal qualification of a Director, and not being
interested in the Society for more than £2 at any
time. It is even a matter of conflicting
evidence whether he did not actually get that
back. Well, Dow was hauled up, with others,
and paraded before a police-court, birst, his
colleagues—Ministers of the Crown, who hold
Police Magistrates in the palms of their hands,
so to speak—gave him a prior testimonial of
innocence, without knowing anything of the
evidence, they gave him their conviction of his
acquittal, if I may be allowed to pun. 1 hat was
bad enough, but the world let it pass without
much murmur. Next, the Court itself (held in
the hollows of the hands aforesaid) acquitted
Dow “without a stain,”&c. He had undoubtedly
assisted by his default to wreck the small
thrift-made fortunes of thousands of poor,
honest nobodies, but that was a small matter.
The world let the Court acquittal also pass
without much murmur. Next, the Gillies
menagerie received their whilom enmeshed
comrade back again witli open arms. This
time, there was a squeak or two from part ql
the press, but nothing to rend the welkin. It
was only feebly urged that a man who signed
away private fools’ savings without much
enquiry and as a matter of form, and when
every single cheque he signed was illegal, wesn t
exactly the man who should be placed over a
department where signing away tracts of the
people’s property as matters of mere form
might come within the routine of his daily
temptations. Well, let that pass, too. But,
now 1 Let the Melbourne Age give us the neat
vault of impudence :
Mv. Charles Sergeant, M.L.0., has consented to act as
treasurer to the testimonial fund that has been voluntarily
inaugurated in various parts of the colony as an expres-
sion of sympathy towards Mr. J. L. Dow, M.L.A., in the
circumstances that led to his temporary retirement from
the position of Minister of Lands. Subscriptions for-
warded to Mr. Sergeant, at Parliament House, will be
handed over to the Central Committee appointed to
supervise the testimonial.
Brass and platinum ! The front of it! Here,
they are going to testimonialise a man for —to
put it in the mildest possible form —stupidly
and carelessly and good-naturedly helping to
ruin 10,000 men better than and as honest as
himself ! Were lin Mr. Dow’s place, my heart
would be riven at the knowledge of the ruin at
which I had so thoughtlessly and unwittingly
assisted, and I should nip this collection busi-
ness in the bud, by imploring Sergeant and
Cos. thus —“For God’s sake, gentlemen, for my
sake, leave that terrible mistake of mine alone.
Let it die. It rankles in my bosom and envenoms
my memory, but let it be limited, please, to
self-torture. Remind others not of that which is
ever present in my remorseful heart. Let my
life atone for this grievous past blindness/’
You have let down Dow very gently, dear
Bulletin, but will you pass over this move
in silence ?If not, I want to know when
Mr. Sergeant is going to start a collec-
tion for the families ruined by the Premier
Permanent follies in which Dow partici-
pated ? I want to know if Dow is manly
enough to forbid such an insult to humanity as
this flaunting testimony—this death-dance over
the grar 7 es of thousands of people’s social hap-
piness? I know that Mr. Dow belongs to a
class of people who hold themselves as being on
a higher plane than ordinary erring mortals —a
class to which also belong Deakin, and Nimmo,
and Mirams, and Mtjnko, and—well, and others
among the sponges who suck nutriment out of
the sentiments and weaknesses of honest man-
hood—the class of water-drinkers and water-
boasters (a water-drinker may be a good man,
but a water-boaster, never !), and every time
this class turns out a specimen of its perfected
type of brazen cheek, I feel humbly thankful
that I am only an honest— Dipsomaniac. —Mel-
bourne, 25th Sept., 1890.
“ The Voice of the Wharf.”
It is a tolerably bad sign of a labour-leader
when the newspapers of the capitalistic classes
are continually loading him with praise. Just
such a species of torture is being meted out
every day in Melbourne to Mr. Hancock, Secre-
tary of the Typographical Association, and
Chairman of the Finance and Control Committee
of the Trades Hall, Melbourne. The Argus
and Daily Telegraph and their satellite
fawners find themselves almost daily impelled
to hold up Mr. Hancock to admiration as a
paragon of broad-mindedness and a level-headed,
calm, shrewd, and honest labour-leader. From
our observation of the gentleman in question,
we don’t think this fulsome praise is nauseous
to him. We have evidence that he is impulsive.
When the Employers in Melbourne held their
first big meeting, Mr. Hancock was present by
invitation. So powerful was his first resultant
impulse that he rushed the newspaper offices
that night with a sample letter to each, in which
he expressed mingled wonder at, and admiration
of the moderate language and demands of the
employers, and made a bold bid for unearned
popularity by suggesting concession. At his
next semi-public utterance —interview with a
Press fiend, if we remember aright— Hancock
was himself again. He had evidently, since his
press-writing frenzy of the night before, fore-
gathered with his unionist mates, and had
received newer and fresher impulses, and so he
scathingly analysed ami criticised the domineer-
ing and dictating speeches of the employers—
the same speeches that he had before found so
full of sweet reasonableness. Mr. Hancock
obtruded anew personality altogether upon the
Victorian Parliament, through the medium of
Mr. Beazley, a member who opposes the open-
ing of libraries and museums on the Sawbath.
Mr. Beazley read to the House a letter from
Hancock, in order to show that Hancock
differed strongly on this Sunday question
from his labour colleague, Trenwith, who is
moving in the direction of getting the people’s
institutions opened to them on the Sabbath. In
this letter, the versatile Hancock pictures him-
self as “ a working-man pleading for this day
of rest ” —an attitude in which he might well
be depicted by some cunning and skilful artist
who wants to make a rapid fortune. Speaking
of Trenwith, Hancock says—
It must not be supposed that the working-men oJ
Victoria are in accord with his religious views. It is
the voice of the wharf and the Hall of Science that you
are invited to listen to.
The mighty Hancock denouncing “ the voice of
the wharf ” hero shows himself as the Victorian
prototype of that fine old New South Wales
Conservative, Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan, M.P.,
who virtuously denies that the New South
Wales workman is a “socialist, a communist,

or a lovelier,” and denounces as “faddists and
fanatics ” the men who say that their object
is to “ re-constitute society.” Yet Hancock
is very glad to get the backing-up of “the
voice of the wharf ” when he organises and
carries on a strike. It is by means of “the
voice of the wharf ” that he hopes to compel
shipowners to do tardy justice to their officers
and seamen ; and squatters to make acceptable
terms with the union shearers. It is this
ignoring of the lower strata of labour, this
creation of exclusive, five-guinea entrance, one-
in-ten-black-ball-pills, trades-union castes, that
constitutes the great weakness of the unions in
time of strike. It is this contempt of the
fundamental principle of a labour-union that
cx’eates a majority of non-unionist labourers,
whom the unions have to fight in addition to
the employers. If his letter is not another
merely tangential impulse, if it expresses his
own contemptuous opinion about “ the voice of
the wharf,” then, we say, that Hancock richly
deserves all the odium which the praise of a
capitalistic press should bring on a masquerading
labour-leader. The voice of the wharf to-day
is the voice of the nation ten years hence.
“ Fire Low!”
The sinful folly of calling amateur swash-
bucklers from the country to mount guard over
a labour crisis in town has been unwittingly
exposed—not that any further exposure was
needed—by a gory-minded individual named
Gordon, who combines the business of news-
paper-proprietor with the pleasure of a lieuten-
ancy in the Mounted Rifles. At the frenzied
call of “duty” Gordon gathered together the
fire-worshippers of Alexandra (Vic.), which is
the mountain home of his weekly organ, and
galloped them away to Melbourne, from whence
he forwarded his editor a yarn that brought the
Yea and Alexandra Standard one transient
gleam of notoriety to brighten the monotony of
its miserable existence. In truth, the tale un-
folded by this newspaper proprietor under arms
will not soon be forgotten. W ith a ludicrous,
though lamentable, 'belief that he was publish-
ing glad tidings, the lieutenant told how
Colonel Price, previous to marching them into
Divine Service, exhorted the Mounted Rifles to
silence any “disturbers of law and order”
(meaning Unionists) by firing low and laying
them out. Colonel Price, when confronted with
this report of his alleged patriotic eloquence,
denied saying “lay them out,” but admitted
that he ordered his braves to fire low and
effectively, because the more effectively they
fired the sooner it would be over. Perhaps,
then, the Yea and Alexandra Standard man
laid them out in his heated imagination—not
that a subtle distinction between words uttered
and meaning conveyed can be of much conse-
quence. W hat we have chiefly to consider is
the spirit that animated the Mounted Rifles in
general, as it was revealed in the lieutenant’s
letter to his own particular rag. On the Sab-
bath morning sacred to the Colonel's harangue
there were 1000 troops in readiness, and “the
knowledge of this state of preparedness for the
maintenance of order had a most salutary effect
on the scourings of humanity ,” chirped the
grim visaged war person who supplies public
opinion to Yea astd Alexandra. Nobody,
as it happened, evinced the least desire
to be fired at low and laid out upon the damp
award of Flinders’ Park, therefore the gallant
boys in barracks had to sit and nurse “ their
one regret ” that they couldn’t have “ a good go-
in. ” The piping times of peace are right enough
for the Mounted Rifle on his native heath, but
when hastily summoned to Melbourne, in the
Queen’s name, he comes with the breezy notion
of having a good go at the scourings of humanity.
In that charitable term any thinking man can
detect at least one mischievous result of the
“ firm policy ’’ adopted by the Governments of
N.S.W. and Victoria. A certain antipathy to
townsmen ever lurks in the dark corners of the
bucolic mind, and needs but little encourage-
ment, unfortunately, to come forth and manifest
itself. If allowed to lie dormant this stupid
antipathy is soon moth-eaten away. The
authorities have just given the accursed thing
an airing, and have sprinkled more pepper of
prejudice over it. It will return to the country
in a comparatively fresh, vigorous condition,
and the idea that a Melbourne labour-crowd
comprises the scourings of humanity will cir-
culate through the interior with the velocity of
a bad shilling.
Special Constables.
Dear Bulletin, —Premier Parkes was right
in a constitutional sense when he rejected the
offer of unionist specials made by unionist
leaders. The quarrel was one between two
sections of the people, neither of which had
rebelled against the State, and the duty of the
Government was, in the first instance, to rely
on its own ordinary resources, and, if these
resources became exhausted, then to appeal to
the general community for auxiliary service for
the preservation of the public peace. But while
Sir Henry was right in conception, he was
lamentably wrong in method. There is no
doubt that had the employers offered a contin-
gent, or the blacklegs offered a contingent, he
would have rejected the offer as he did that of
the unionists ; for the principle was a clear one,
and could not have been violated without in-
curring national reproach. But, in effect, Sir
Henry s action stultifies the principle. In the
first place, he did not sufficiently rely on the
ordinary resources of the Government. Although
in his official capacity currents of information
reach him which do not come to the average
citizen, it is still safe to hold that he ordered
the enrolment of special constables before the
ordinary resources were exhausted. Every
citizen car. rorm an idea upon that, and,
merely remarking that special means of
obtaining news are often special means
of exciting panic in the receiver, I let this
{toint drop. On the second point 1 impeach
>ir Henry with being guilty of that form of
political blunder which is always a crime. The
method adopted by him to obtain special con-
stables put the special constabulary into the
employers’ hand. The employers or their
devoted myrmidons donned the badge and
gripped the baton of authority, ostensibly to
r *strain excesses, but in reality to show unionists
that the employers had the Government at their
back. This was a political, a social crime. It
might have led to flaming houses and gutters of
blood. In a free country and when Labour is
stronger such a blind act on the part of an
administrator will, beyond a doubt, turn a feud
between two sections of the public into a war to
the knife between one of the sections and
the State. Sir Henry throws the blame, in
so far as he is willing to admit blame, for
the fact that the unionists are not as
well represented among the specials as are the
employers, on Mr. Eosbery. As Colonial
Secretary he considers that he did his duty
when he laid down a principle, and if the work-
ing out of the principle gave the employers a
special constabulary, he alleges that to be a
matter of detail with which he should not be
expected to be acquainted. But in a case like
the present the detail is the heart of the prin-
ciple, and when in the enrolment any one
section showed an intention to preponderate, it
was the bounden duty of Government to frus-
trate that intention. Dereliction of duty on this
point is a crime—and in certain circumstances
one which the criminals do not get a second
opportunity to perpetrate. It is to be hoped
that when the trouble subsides, a return show-
ing the occupations of the present batch of
special constables will be laid on the table of
the Legislative Assembly.—Yours, Onlooker.
“I Want to go Home.’'
The exodus of vice-regal ladies from this un-
happy country is, in the language of journalism,
assuming the preportions of an epidemic. The
Countess of Hopetoun, an apparently healthy
enchantress, who came up smiling at Randwick
races only the other day, “ has been causing
her Earl much uneasiness” {runs the official
narrative) for some time past, and is sailing for
England on October 11th in order to get the
‘ ‘ best London advice ” re her physical condition,
and the latest fashions. Hopetoun himself we
had regarded as a noble object of uneasiness
since he swooned over his first leim, but our
fears were evidently misplaced, inasmuch as his
fascinating consort was the really delicate
member of the firm. Doubtless he fainted at
the thought of losing her, and it is to
avoid such a catastrophe that the lady’s
pilgrimage to dear old Hingland was ordered
by the faculty. Anyhow, the Victorian Governor-
ess intends to go where Lady Norman has
gone, and where Lady Kintore is expected to
be found shortly. We have a hazy notion
(unfortunately our recollections of these great
social events are considerably mixed) that Lady
Onslow has also sailed, or wants to sail, for the
land of her birth, and we are prepared to take
oath that Lady Robinson got there a good deal
during her husband’s reign in South Australia.
Here, then, we have enough solid fact and hazy
supposition to justify an anxious enquiry as to
whether this hemisphere can be expected to
hold together much longer? We find it stated
in all directions that the nominal Governor of
any Australasian colony is merely intended to
form a respectable background for his exalted
missus, and that the actual representative of
Victoria Regina is a sweet vision in petticoats
upon whose successful mashing of the populace
the strengthening of “ those silken bonds ”
depends. Lord Knutseord has said as much
the press on both sides of the world commended
the Colonial Secretary’s candour. An ex-Gover-
nor recently wrote in the National Review
that a pretty and accomplished wife was an
essential for every deputy monarch in these
parts. What then is the position of Lord Hope-
toun or Sir Robinson, or Sir Norman, or any
other such when his essential packs up her
bonnets and things and cuts the country ? He
becomes practically useless. He is but the dis-
consolate shadow of a vanished substance, and
yet this empty apparition draws his salary as
though he were still a great vital principle.
The matter must be looked into rightaway. If
a so-called Governor is billeted on us for the
sake of his wife, in whom all the important
functions of vice-regality are vested, what’s the
good of him as a grass-widower ? Rise up, Aus-
tralia ! Stretch your hand across the sea and
clench it in the face of Knutseord, or some-
body, until he explains why the genuine Gover-
nors are allowed to leave us without any visible
means of social, political and Imperial support
except an exploded fiction in trousers who grabs
the bawbees of a deserted people.
*
Loaded Dice.
Smidt, the foreigner, tried at Wagga Circuit
Court for murdering John Young Taylor, has
been in custody ever so many months, and has
all the while been most effectively prosecuted
by the police and the newspapers; we have
even seen it alleged in the press that a gaol
official (who, so far as we can perceive, has not
taken the trouble to deny the reports), has
been letting his tongue run outside concerning
certain discrepancies between statements made
by prisoner at different dates since his arrest.
And yet, when this miserable Smidt was
arraigned it was officially discovered that he
had no money wherewith to pay for his
defence, and the Crown then—at 11.59 J
p.m., so to speak—assigned him the ser-
vices of a junior barrister. As the Crown
must have known all along that the prisoner
was a pauper, and that he was locked up in
gaol without the chance of communicating
(save through the medium of the prosecution’s
henchman, the police) with any witnesses he
might have to call, it might surely have had the
decency, the humanity, to afford him earlier
facilities for preparing to cast the dice of Law.
The brief for the prosecution was no doubt
ready weeks ago. The theory is that the State
has no especial wish for the conviction of any
individual, and that it only desires to sec
justice done. How, in the name of all that is
true and manly, can justice be done in the cir-
cumstances under which Smidt appeared at the
bar? To treat thus a man accused of a capital
crime is infamous ; it is a relic of barbarism to
which the public are unhappily accustomed, but
which, could they regard it with any but the
eyes of hoary precedent, would be scouted as a
revolting crime. Smidt is understood to be a
German, and if this be liqw does it happen
that we db not find the German Consul-Genera!
protesting against the brutal treatment meted
out to this foredoomed citizen of his powerful
country ? There was at one time in Sydney a
generous man (his name was never divulged)
who, out of his own pocket, provided proper
means of defence for every moneyless prisoner
on trial for his life. That philanthropist is now
dead and gone. Is the State too mean to stand
in his shoes ?
John Bellin, the Melbourne auctioneer, sits
limp and desolate in his dungeon cell, wonder-
ing why the Law sentenced him to five years
imprisonment, instead of condemning him to the
healthful three-months' seclusion allotted to
Town-councillor Geddes. The two cases bare a
strong family resemblance up to a certain point.
Both Geddes and Bet.t.tn were paragons of
prayer and respectability, and each took ad-
vantage of his position to embezzle the funds of
certain societies “ without mentioning of it”—as
Coombes in “ The Silver King ” would say. In
the subsequent matter of tearful repentance there
was nothing to choose between these misguided
lambs. Their unanimity of regret was no less
remarkable than touching. Bellin, however,
disgraced himself by merely filching as many
hundreds of pounds as Geddes sneaked thou-
sands, and as he furthermore neglected to be a
drunken Rechabite his resemblance to the other
shining light may be said to have broken down
at two mitigating circumstances. Thus it
happened, doubtless, that Bellin got five year's,
whilst Geddes was called upon to pay only five
per cent, of that penalty, and the former is
herewith exhorted to take his skilly in a spirit
of resignation and acknowledge his sin in
embezzling so little by comparison with the
Municipal Rechabite who kept his bottle on the
chimbley-piece and put his, lips to it when so
dispoged.
The “Book-Purchasers’ Protection Act” just
passed by the New South Wales Legislature,
contains the following clause, moved by an
attorney:—
In any action hereafter arising in any Court on any
contract for the purchase of such printed matter whether
entered into before or after the passing of this Act, the
Court may determine the value of the said printed matter,
proof of which shall be on the vendor.
In other words, the Act says that where a pur-
chaser has contracted in writing to buy a book
at a certain stated price and to pay for it on
delivery, he is at liberty to appeal to a Court
and ask it to fix a lower price. This clumsy
and immoral piece of retrospective legislation
will, The Bulletin predicts, give rise to much
unprofitable litigation. How can the vendor
prove the “ value ”of his book ? How can the
value of a book be proved at all ? It is as un-
certain a quantity as the value of a picture, and
as no two men acting independently are likely
either to assess a book or a picture at exactly
the same price, so no two Courts are likely to
agree on such a matter. It does not follow
that because £IO,OOO has been spent in pro-
ducing a book that the book is valuable (for
the producer of it may have been a gilded
idiot), any more than it follows that
because a book only cost £SOO to bring
out it is worth less than the book whose
cost was £IO,OOO. Suppose it is admitted
by defendant that he ordered the book, and the
Court considers the value of same to be over the
amount claimed, will the value in excess of the
claim be awarded to plaintiff? Many of the
books now sold by subscription are not even
written when they are sold to the public, and
supposing that they are produced according to
the written agreement between the parties, is
the defendant to be released from his engage-
ment merely because he has consented to buy a
pig in a poke ? The Book Purchasers’ Protec-
tion Act is clearly in conflict with the common
law. Which law is to prevail ?
Perhaps the saddest sight on earth is a fat
woman trying to look cute.
“ It must be admitted that the Shearers’
Union is a magnificently solid institution,” said a
well-known squatter the other day in Brisbane.
“From what I know of these fellows, I am cer-
tain that if it would help any cause they have
made their own, every man Jack of them would
strip himself of his shirt and a big hunk of the
skin.”
If asked to drink, call for Wolfe’s Schnapps.
The proposed Sydney Domestic Servants’
Union rules provide, it is alleged, for eight hours’
work, beginning at 9 a.m.~and therefore knocking
out early morning tea and all such tyrannies. After-
noon tea is also abolished in favour of Bridget’s
afternoon nap. The placing of anything under
lock and key is strictly prohibited. Every even-
ing is an evening out, and whenever the missus
becomes ill she has to provide a paid hospital-
nurse.
Wolfe’s Schnapps, the purest spirit procurable
Chief Justice Lilley, of Queensland, writes
to a Brisbane paper to complain that his name
has been placed without his permission on the
members’ list of the “ National Conservative
Club,” England. The democratic judge strongly
objects to this “Imperialistic kidnapping,” as he
styles it, this association of the Republican Lilley
with the Monarchical Primrose, and imagines
that this insult to him and his principles has come
from the hands of some “returned” Australian
cad, for the purpose of convicting him of incon-
sistency and discrediting Australian democracy, or
the Judge himself, by a false pretence of expulsion
under the Club’s 10th rule for “conduct in oppo-
sition to the fundamental political principle upon
which the club has been established.” The judge
has “ requested his associate to inform the club
that I have never applied for membership with
them, nor authorised anyone to apply on my
behalf; that lam not a Conservative, and cer-
tainly not an English Conservative, but an Aus-
tralian Nationalist who believes in the future
absolute independence of his country, under no
crown or coronet, but under a government of her
people, by her people, for her people.”

Notwithstanding the unqualified assertion
of the S.M. Herald’s critic No. 2, that Meroy
Merrick is Janet Aohurohs best Sydney imper-
sanation, we are bold enough to style it her worst
and a bad worst, too, for none of its previous im’
personators were gifted with Miss Aohuroh’s
natural endowments, but each gave a less artificial
and more coherent reading. Here and there her
acting was faultless, but just as folks got inter-
ested she would rant like a barn-stormer, rush
round and round the stage like a caged animal,
snort like a halter-hung horse, or work her features
like one strychnine-poisoned. The possibilities of
a magnificent future are being wrecked for want
of intelligent advice and a strong controlling hand.
As Horace Holmoroft. Herbert Flemming acted as
though he thought the part scarcely worthy of his
attention, consequently the audience formed the
opinion at an early stage of the proceedings that
if he were in love with anyone in particular it was
himself, and didn’t feel at all astonished when be
jilted the New Magdalen. Charles Charrington
made desperate efforts to be effective as Julian
Gray, and several of his speeches were creditable
elocutionary efforts, but his curate was of the
carpet- slipper order, not of the muscular
Christian hero brand, and the actor too evi-
dently lacks the training necessary to the
attainment of success in such a character. We
are glad to see that several of the critics
are outspoken enough to acknowledge that Roland
Watts-Phillips’ Grace Rosebery is the best im-
personation of the play ; it is more than that, it is
a very excellent piece of acting. Mabel Tracey is
not well placed as Lady Janet Roy, but Stirling
White, Henry Hoyte, and Frank Harcourt, were
effective in the small parts of the prologue. “ The
Long Strike ” by the late Boucicault, will be
staged at Her Majesty’s on Saturday, when
William Rignold will impersonate the mill-hand
with the dialect of which he is such a master.

The production of “ Chilperic ” having
been delayed until Wednesday owing to the
necessity for further rehearsal, not the less worthy
motive that has been rumoured, “Paul Jones”
was revived for six nights to business slightly
better than that prevailing at the tail-end of the
“Boccaccio” run. The name-part was taken
during the first three nights by Marian Burton,
and during the last three by Sydney Deane, who
was fairly successful. But his popularity would
scarcely sustain the test of a long run in the
character.
** * *
Janet Achurch takes a benefit on Saturday
afternoon, at the Criterion Theatre, when she will
play in two acts of “Camille,” the 8.8. crowd
will play an act of “ D.D.,” Jewett and Oatheart
will put up a scene from “Julius Csesar,” and M.
Poussard, Harry Rickards, and many others will
perform. It is hard to say why Mrs. Charring-
ton should ask—why the public should accord her—
a benefit. During the last three months she has
earned more money than many worthy local per-
formers have in the last five years. Under such
circumstances, in every case it would be more
straightforward for the favourites of fortune to
come before the theatre-going public purely as the
vendors of acceptable theatrical goods and not as
beseeehers for eleemosynary bounty.

The Harry Rickards Co. continue to fill
Sydney School of Arts nightly. The lately-engaged
ballad-singer, Rita Dale, has a fresh and lusty
voice which only requires careful training to be-
come a valuable property. The bagpipe solos of
the kilted Highland dancer, T. M. Mackay, are
somewhat soul-satisfying, but the much-medalled
and high-kilted gentleman has caught on and, as
an evidence of his popularity, the “ bald-heads”
row ” is nightly filled with members of the fair
sex. The entrepreneur’s new songs “ Hard Times”
and “How he Carries On” are likely to become
popular. In order to rest Mrs. Rickards, May
Holt joins (the ninth new-comer) to play “Nan,
the Good-for-nothing. ”

ringers are now performing daily at Bondi
Aquarium. The sensation of the season comes off

Viola, a lady who has previously gone “up”
under other names, will soar into the blue em-

pated. A new company have obtained possession
of Bondi Aquarium, and lots of summer novelties
are promised. Manager Wyburd, however, still
remains to hump round the plate on Sundays.

The fifth performance of Hazon's Orchestra
in Sydney Centennial Hall was made highly
interesting by the appearance of a French singer,
Jd. 0. Deslouis, who received a magnificent recep-
tion and is the owner of a highly-cultured bari-
tone especially good in the upper register, though
its effect to Australian ears is impaired by the use
Of the vibrato ; also by the performance of a
gavotte, Composed by W. C. Browne (son of the
former member for Patrick’s Plains in the N.S. W.
Assembly), an Australian native, and dedicated
to Mons. Kowalski by the composer, his pupil.
The composition—albeit somewhat reminisoential
and disfigured by an abrupt “ break ’ -—was
onthusiastically encored and is more pleasing than
some trifles of a similar character that have come
to us over well-known names.

The “ attractive novelty ” which the sar-
castic R. S. Smythe promised for the Best farewell
recitals has proved a real mascotte. Marian
Burton must never return to comic opera. As she
ensured the success of Santley’s last Melbourne
season, so the attractiveness of Best’s farewell
performances in Sydney, though given at higher
prices, is enhanced to such an extent by her
charming singing that the Centennial Hall is not
nearly large enough for the crowds who went to
hear her. She will be received with open arms in
New Zealand, whither she shortly proceeds under
the management of the “ Much-travelled. ”

The dapper Gaul who scowls or smirks,
The lord by “ Tottie” led,
The pretty, pallid girl who works
To earn her honest bread ;
The cheeky Phyllis fresh and neat,
With feller flash and gay,
The grimy Arab of the street —
They all are types of folks we meet
In London, Day by Day.
The Hebrew with historic nose.
Friend Pat upon the spree,
The hero good who homeward goes
Each evening to tea ;
The scornful, dark, deserted wife
Whose story’s “like a play ”
They jostle in the busy strife
For riches, pleasure, fame, or life
In London, Day by Day.
No fitter place, in any age.
To serve us as the scene
Of human passion on the stage
Than this has ever been ;
There Lazarus dies at the gate
Of Dives, sad to say ;
Three millions fight with stubborn fate,
Love, envy, pity, curse, or hate,
Despair too soon, or watch and wait.
What joys intense and sorrows great
Are strangely mingled—early, late —
In London, Day by Day I
* * «
Bland Holt’s latest production at Mel-
bourne Alexandra can hardly fail to bring back
more than the big bagfull of shekels expended
upon it. “ London, Day by Day ” contains enough
fun, trouble, character, story, and scenery for two
melodramas of the modern style. _ The plot jumps
off from scratch, and keeps going at top-speed
until it breasts the tape, instead of stopping to get
second breath, or occasionally wandering off the
track and losing itself. Likening the company of
thirty-one performers to Price’s Mounted
they may be said to “aim low and effectively,
with the result that the audience are “laid out’
in admiring heaps and have to be dragged away
from the scene of slaughter. These thirty-one
creations of Sims and Pet ti f t form a representa-
tive cockney combination, with an innocent
ticket-of-le&ve girl at the head of affairs, ihe
usual untold gold falls due to the afflicted pansy,
and an evil-minded foreigner wants to marry her
in consequence, but his deserted wife goes around
warning people until he strews the floor with her
remains, after which vengeance pursues the wrong
individual for a while previous to collaring the
man of sin and leaving the other fellow in the
arms of the first girl. A gaudy Yiddisher plays a
cheerful hand in most of the wickedness, and most
of the others evince a rollicking disposition, unless
they happen to be magistrates, or policemen, or
mere “citizens,” in which cases they incline to
gloom. The first of eleven scenes is laid in
Jermyn-street, the last at St. Katherine’s Docks.
They are generally within the radius of Covent
Garden’s vegetable aroma, and not one goes fur-
ther afield than Hampton Court. Therefore,
'* London, Day by Day ” hangs together better
than dramas which stretch from Middlesex to
Egvpt and require the leading characters to meet
one another promiscuous-like in about thirteen
different regions.
#
The average strength of acting is remark-
able, considering that two dozen and seven souls
have a say in the matter, albeit the say of some is
brief. Of the principals Bland Holt, Baker and
Maggie Knight, are better than Walter Howe,
Albert Norman and Mrs. Bland Holt. The latter
was never cut out for a cynical deserted wife,
the men suffer from too much front teeth and a
superfluity of eye respectively. Polly Emery is
quite excellent as a gutter-snipe, pretty Virginia
Vivienne seems to be coming on nicely, and her
stage suitor is fairly deserving of such a delicious
mash. H. Plummer, although lacking in repose
and shirtcuff, strikes one as a promising young
man, and a more or less favourable mention of the
remaining two dozen is crowded out.
