Uncle Tom-s Cabin,” as now revived at
the Opewa House, is a sad and dreary affair in
most parts and a somewhat trying entertainment
in others. The old boss nigger himself is a less
genuine kind of nuisance than of yore, the slave-
hunters arc rather short of that cussodncss which
formerly gave them tone, and the humour Of
lawyer Marks has lost its bloom, Little Eva, too.
ia a ghastly mockery under present conditions*
for whereas she starts off by being a real child, the
consumptive innocent subsequently turns up as
Graoie Whiteford, whose infancy is of an
apocryphal character entirely, more especially
when she sings. The plantation niggers hold
Divine Services and chortle such merry glees as
“Roll, Jordan, Roll,” or “Stealing away,”
until yawning sinners in front would fam
take offertory dishes round to relieve the mono-
tony. There is no sense in “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin. ’
Everybody now knows, or ought to know, that it
is founded on the fictions of a disagreeable old
woman, and the world shrewdly suspects that
poor Eliza bolted from a kind master to be
whacked to death by George Harris. -

Attenuated audiences still dot themselves
over the desert vastness of the Royal, but
“Famine” and Hubert O’Grady are to be with-
drawn after Friday. A piece of idiocy not
generally noticed is the calling a girl “ Lady
Alice” because her father is a baronet. O’Grady’s
ignorance of the laws relating to “handles” is
nothing much against him, but the fact that he
hadn’t been advised to remove such a glaring error
from the playbill makes one speculate upon the
class of audience he writes dramas for. “Lady
Alice ” (daughter of Sir Richard) gives the author
clean away in one line. On Saturday Mr. Martin

One good turn deserves another, and
occasionally gets it. Brough and Boucicault have
set apart Friday (Got. 3rd) for a benefit to George
C. Miln, at Melbourne Bijou, on which interesting
occasion the chief items in a powerful bill will be
the Examination Act, from “ School,” the
first thrill of “Robbery Under Arms,” the
Trial Scene from “Merchant of Venice,” and
“The Goose with the Golden Eggs,” an aged
farce which offers Elton and Royce an
opportunity to chip in their services. His
Hexcellency has contributed his radiant patronage,
and the ghost of Shakespeare is expected to drop
in during the evening to express his immortal
approval of Miln’s plucky efforts to popularise
classic drama amongst a leg-loving people, ihe
tragedian’s next move is to be in the direction of
India’s coral strands, and after he has rounded off
Friday’s performance with a spaohe, his Mel-
bourne friends will wave a fond adieu.
** * *
G. 0, Miln was examined in Melbourne
Insolvency Court on Monday. They couldn’t
stop him when he once started on his own mis-
fortunes. Here are a few gems from a long ex-
planation delivered in tragic and pathetic tones.
Brisbane was a brilliant success—£'ls after six
weeks in a tropical climate. Sydney was a failure,
and he cleared out as quickly as he could. No
one wanted him now. A Shaksperian in the
Australian colonies was like the Great Eastern
ten miles inland. Melbourne Opera House was a
first-class theatrical coffin. The syndicate that
launched him in Tasmania gave him £2O, and
pocketed over £2OO. Certainly, his wife drew a
salary. What an absurd question ! She was
one. of the best leading ladies in Aus-
tralia. He couldn’t say what he made
out of “Othello” in Melbourne. No, not m this
world or the next. But he hoped to meet counsel
for the trustee in the next world, all the same. A
lot of fellows with blue papers used to wait for
him outside the stage-door in Melbourne. He
never had any desire to see them. All he wanted
was to go and get a living and be square with the
world. That was the gist of it. The moral is
obvious. Miln will probably play in Adelaide.
He swears he’ll never dodge his creditors. I hey 11
be told all his movements.

Luscombe Searelle’s “ new ” comic opera,
“The Black Rover,” which he is producing in
London, appears to be simply “ Isadora ” under
another name. The latter play he brought out at
Sydney Opera House about five years ago. If
we remem D6r rightly, the public didn t hanker
much after “Isadora,” which ran for one conse-
cutive week. The leading parts were held by
Grade Plaisted, Lithgow James, and Oily Peer-
ing—the latter playing an old imbecile whose age
increased each time he made his appearance until
ultimately he announced “he was over 10 million
years old,” and then the curtain fell. A dreamy
hymn-tune, which was dragged in about three
times in each act, "was the only number in the
opera that “ caught on ” at all.

If the ordinary cablegram-manufacturer
can out-do Ananias, where does the theatrical
cablegram-manufacturer come in. and howoften are
the messages sent by the artistes whose tremendous
success they record, themselves ? On theinormng
after the first production in Melbourne some time
ago of a new opera bouffe, a certain lady who
played a principal part was with her husband in
the Bourke - street telegraph - office. As they
took no trouble to modulate their voices, an
audience of some dozen were speedily aware that
it was a question of dispatching a cablegram to
London recording the event of the previous night.
After a protracted discussion, in which, however,
hubby took little part, the fair lady decided that
the only expression equal to the occasion was
“unparalleled success,” and, with many lingering
looks and an evident desire to put in a little more
jam, this was eventually sent off. Charley
Warner and Emily Eitzroy, are you listening ?

Mrs. Brown-Potter found herself in front
of a serious difficulty on the last night of
“Hero and Leander ” at Melbourne Princess’.
She was doing a forlorn soliloquy about the
absence of Leander. who had gone out to have an
ancient Greek drink, or was otherwise unavoid-
ably absent; but just at the most forlorn part of
the atory Leander himself put his head in at the
window. He was nearly ten minutes ahead of
time, so the weeping maidof Greece calmly ignored
the fact that he was there, and kept on asking
why he didn’t come. There was just two feet of
space between them, but Hero openly stated that
he wan far, far away, and gave way to grief over
his absence. Then the gallery hollered out that he
was just a-coming in at the window, and Leander
smiled a harassed smile, but Hero went on wit a
her alarmingly improbable statements. i tea
yer he’s gittin’ in at the window,” said the clarion
voice from near the roof, and Leander looked as it
he wished be was dead, and didn’t know whether
to hang on or simply state that he would call m
later. There was no joy in the meeting that night;.
Leander had caught the wrong steamer from Asia
and spoiled things.

The house is nightly packed as if with potatoes in
a sack, and the press has fairly gushed over her
curly juvenile. The alleged criticism of the Ad-
vertiser was unqualified slobber from leading line
to wind up; and had a strong managerial twain?
about it, heightened by a soupgon of Sillery and
fat natives. The Register, in discreetly worded
criticisms, certainly suggests, in a half-hearted
way, that Mrs. B.P. is not a heaven-born actress,
but tins assertion is so qualified with “ buts ” and
“ ifa ” and so overlaid with jam that the outside
public are completely mystified. Meantime, with

is good.

During one of the Bairnsfather entertainments lately,
at the close of the evening the company sang “ Auld Lang
Syne,” and when they came to the verse beginning ‘ And
surely you’ll be your pint stoup,” an enthusiastic country-
man stood up and advanced to the front of the stage,
holding aloft a glass and a bottle of mountain dew, which
he offered to Mr. Bairnsfather. The latter accepted the
offer and, shaking hands, drank, amidst the cheers of
the audience, the affair putting everyone in the best of
humour.

“ Lenton ” is the name he wishes to have attached to his
works during his lifetime, and inscribed upon his marble
obelisk when he is no more. Albert Smith used to tell a
story of a Swiss village postmaster who retained scores of
letters addressed to English tourists, on the ground that
they were intended for Monsieur, Esq., whose neglect to
call for his correspondence was a constant source of irrita-
tion to the local guardian.

In 1828, when “ Macbeth” was produced at the Theatre
Royal Sudbury, Eng., the audience included a man who
had been to Bury St. Edmunds to witness the execution
of William Corder for the murder of the girl Maria
Marten. This spectator of the execution had taken
refuge in the theatre to avoid the importunities of people
for a full and particular account of what he had seen, for
there prevailed a general belief that the man would not be
hanged. Just as the man had taken his seat the fourth
scene in the first act was commencing, and he was quietly
seating himself in a box near the stage, when Duncan
be cum, in the words of the author, “Is execution done on
Cawdor ?” “ Yes, sir,” said the man, “ I saw him hanged
this morning—and that’s the last time I’ll answer any
more questions about it 1”
Dear Bulletin, —Those who are looking forward to the
approaching visit of Sarah Bernhardt must not expect te
see the great actress as she was, but must remember that
we never get them in their prime. Age and a me
orageuse ” have done their work now. The beautiful voice
the great actress’s greatest charm —is already a thing of
the past, and, although the old fire may remain, it will
probably be intermittent. Still, the critics are doubtless
preparing to exhaust their vocabulary of superlatives..
And, after all, she may not come—for another five years.
—An Old Hand.

recently received some curiously-worded messages from
Lockwood, the manager of his touring company, whose
practice it is to send a telegram every evening, of which
the first words give the receipts in cipher, while other
intelligence is conveyed in plain terms. Here is a speoi-
men Miss Collier wants white tights, the
explanation of this being N M, the letters denoting the
receipts, and Collier (the wardrobe mistress) wants white
tights Lockwood, wishing to augment the company,
wired Lin card “Must have three more girls in chorus,
and if Faithfull goes four.” (Beatrice Faithfull was about
to rejoin the “ Old Guard” company at the Grand Theatre,
Leeds.) The meesage was received as follows: —“Must
have three more girls in chorus, and if faithful ones four.”

pany) has filled his pants well.” “ Pants,” of course,
should have read “ parts.”

The Knights of Labour are already 1500
strong in New Zealand, and the body recently
formed in Victoria is daily adding to its numbers.
They advocate the formation of a political labour
party, the imposition of a tax on community-
created land values, and the inauguration of a co-
operative system.

At the lately-concluded Sydney Quarte;
Sessions, which, by the way, are held about
monthly, Judge MTarland passed in review 98
prisoners, of whom 27 were acquitted. Some two
or three were remanded, but the great majority
were “sent up’’for terms varying from 10 years
downwards. The prisoners were of all ages and
sizes, from the boy of 10 in knickerbockers, who
got six months for breaking and entering, to the
man of 70, who got 10 years for a similar offence.
Twenty-one prisoners received sentences of from
two months to two years ; 13 got three years each ;
14 got four years; nine got five years; one got
six; two got seven; one got eight; and one got
10 years. The total duration of sentences
amounted to over 206 years.

“The Conventional Lies
of our Civilisation.”
THE ECONOMIC LIE.— CHAPTER II. CONTINUED.
BY MAX NORDAU.
Thk college graduate thinks himself of too much
account to descend into and be lost in the lowest
class of society, by voluntarily assuming the trade
of a manual-labourer, and according to the ideas
prevalent in society he is correct. He demands of
the world that he be supported as a master, not
support himself, like a slave. But the world has
only a limited demand for the kind of work which
the college-bred man considers suitable for him.
Hence, in the older civilised countries, at least
one-half of the graduates are condemned to spend
their lives in hoping and envying, obtaining none
of life’s blessings, fighting hard for the small
amount of daily bread they require, and often
going hungry, standing beside the over-loaded,
groaning table of the upper ten thousand, while
suffering the pangs of semi-starvation. Certain
friends of humanity, of that stamp who consider
wars and pestilences as blessings for the human
race, because they leave more room and better
conditions of existence for those remaining alive,
these people express their convictions that culti-
vation is an injury to mankind, that the increase
in the number of intermediate and high schools is
an attempt to destroy the happiness of the masses,
because in them more discontented professional
failures, future barricade-fighters aud dynamiters
are being raised and let loose upon the community.
As things are now these reasoners are in the
right. As long as the college-bred young man con-
siders himself disgraced by manual labour, because
the labourer is despised, as long as he sees in his
diploma an instrument by which to compel society
to rally to his support, and as long as he considers
himself entitled by his education to the parasitic
life of the wealthy classes —as long as these con-
ditions endure, his education will bring him far
more unhappiness, in five cases out of ten, than he
would ever experience if he were without it and
leading the life of a handicraftsman or even of a
day-labourer.
* * *
This can only be remedied by giving back to
education its natural role. It must be its own
object. We must learn to consider that a culti-
tivated mind is, in itself, a sufficient reward for the
efforts made to get the cultivation, that we have
no right to expect any other reward for these
efforts, and that its possession does not relieve us
in any way from the duty of productive labour. A
cultivated mind has a fuller and richer conscious-
ness of its Ego, it grasps better the phenomena of
the world and of life, it can appreciate and enjoy
the beauties of art and of literature, and its exis-
tence gives its possessor a far more liberal and in-
tensive life in every respect than that led by the
ignorant. We are ungrateful if, in addition to
these priceless blessings for the inner life, we
demand of education that it should also provide
us with our daily bread : this should be the task
of our hands. But if, on one hand, the man of
culture ought not to despise the immediate pro-
duction of articles for the market, society, on the
other hand, should make education accessible to
all those capable of receiving and profiting by it.
Compulsory school attendance is only a weak be-
ginning. How can poor men afford to send their
children to school until they are 10 or 12 years old,
when they are unable to feed and clothe them
during that time, and the little ones must labour
for their own support ? And is it justifiable, is it
consistent, for the State to say : “ You must learn
to read and write; thus far shalt thou go and no
farther?” Why does compulsory school attend-
ance cease at the elementary grades ? Why does
it not extend to the higher branches ? Ignorance
is either an infirmity in the individual and conse-
quently in the community, or else it is not. If it
is no infirmity, why are the children compelled to
attend the primary and elementary schools? If it
is, why is it not cured completely by a complete
and rounded education ? Is not knowledge of the
laws of nature as valuable as the multiplication-
table ? The coming voters, in whose hands lie the
destinies of their native land, do not they need
any acquaintance with history, politics and
national economy ? Can they get the full benefit
of the art of reading which they have mastered
if they are not instructed in, nor even introduced
to, the master-pieces of prose and poetry in their
national literature? The intermediate schools
provide for this, at least. Why, then, is not
attendance upon the intermediate schools made
compulsory ? The obstacle is a material one. The
poor man who has already experienced great
difficulty in supporting his child until he graduates
from the primary school, would find it utterly im-
possible to carry the burden of his maintenance
until he had reached an advanced age, until his
eighteenth or twentieth year. He is compelled
by sheer necessity to convert the labouring
power of his child into money at the
earliest possible moment. In order to have
the benefits of the intermediate schools shared
by as many pupils as attend the primary schools,
the labour of the scholars should be organised and
utilised, as is the case in some educational insti-
tutions in the United States, where the pupils
carry on a farm or work at some manual trade, in
connection v/(Jh their studies, with sufficient
success and pecuniary returns, aided by outside
benevolent contributions to a certain extent, to
support themselves during their school-life. A
far better and more consistent plan would be for
the community to supply not only instruction but
the entire material support of the scholars during
their years of study. “ That would be pure Com-
munism !” exclaims some obstinate adherent of
that organised egotism which we call the existing
science of political economy.
# * *
I might flatter him by disclaiming the horrid
word and saying : “No, that would not be Com-
munism, but the solidarity of the community.”
But I disdain to pJay hide-and-seek with thought,
and thus I say frankly : Yes, it would be a bit of
Communism. But are we not living in a complete
state of Communism ? Is it not Communism for
the State to provide compulsory education for the
whole generation of children from their sixth to
their twelfth year ? Is not the mental food thus
provided for them one kind of food ? Does it not
cost money ? Is it not the community which
supplies this money? And the standing army?
Is not this also founded upon pure Communism ?
Does not the community support in this way a
whole generation of young men between their
twentieth and twenty-third year, and not with
mental food alone, but with actual food and cloth-
ing, house and home ? Why should it be more diffi-
cult or more unreasonable for the State to support
a million children during their entire school-life as
far as the university, than to support half a million
young men during their years of military service ? #
The expense? It would be no greater than the
expense of keeping up the army. And the main-
tenance and development of an army is of no
greater importance to the safety and prosperity of
the nation than the more complete education of
the generation growing up around us. And
besides, why cannot the two aims be combined ?
Why cannot the State feed and clothe the entire
male generation until the seventeenth or
eighteenth year as it now feeds and clothes the
regular army, and during this time in connection
with the primary and intermediate schooling
given to them let them be receiving their military
instruction ? The national labour would gain
vastly by the substitution of the less costly arms
of the scholar-soldiers for those of the strong and
trained young men of .twenty to twenty-three, of
whose valuable labour the community is now
deprived. The actual gain in this way to the
nation would represent an amount of money suffi-
cient to cover the entire extra expense of the
soldier army over the present army, whose
capability for labour is condemned to three years
of unproductiveness, at the very blossoming-time
of its development.
* * *
Such a system, to be complete, must be founded
upon a certain other con-
dition. Not every mine
is capable of receiving and
assimilating the higher
and highest branches of
learning. If the State is
to take charge of the
whole population of
scholars throughout the
country and thus make
education possible to all,
even to the son of the
poorest man, then it must
take care that its bene-
fits are not wasted upon
those who are unworthy
or incapable of profiting
by them. At the close
of each school year a
strict and exhaustive ex-
amination of the scholars
should take place, and
those only be allowed
to enter the grade above
who were able to sustain
the examination. In this
way the talentless scholar
would drop out of school
after having acquired the
elementary branches,
which are as much of a
mental load as he is
capable of bearing; the
mediocre intelligence
would leave school after
having acquired part or
the whole of the interme-
diate branches, while
only the pupils possessing
real talent would work
their way into the highest
educational
the scientific, technical
and art academies. By
this system liberal educa-
tion would become the
property of the entire
people; instead of being,
as it is now, a privilege
only enjoyed by the
wealthy classes. Manual
labour would be no longer
synonymous with lack of
'fO’.:> a
If i?SS t'C*&
iAf3>r f\ r
cultivation, and theedueated youngman would incur
no disgrace if he earned his livelihood by the
direct production of articles from the market.
The overcrowding of the liberal professions by
presuming and unauthorised mediocrities would be
prevented. Genuine talent that had been obliged
to display and prove its authenticity and its claim
to the title in a dozen competitive examinations
of constantly increasing severity would find in its
diploma the absolute guarantee of an honourable
livelihood ; the problematic existences would dis-
appear and shabby gentility cease to exist. This
system would thus be found a complete cure for
one of the most dangerous wounds in the body of
society.
* * *
One picture of the political economy of our civi-
lisation in the preceding pages included the
privileged classes, the men of wealth and leisure,
who live on the labour of others, the group of
college-bred young men who consider that their
possession of a diploma entitles them to live the
life of a parasite on the working classes, the same
as the millionaire’s wealth entitles him, and the
proletaires, the lowest class in society, torn from
the soil intended by nature to support man, with-
out property of any kind, toiling for a mere sub-
sistence. What a tragic figure in the midst of our
civilisation ! What a pregnant criticism of the
world’s progress, this factory employe ! The lines
are often quoted in which La Bruyere describes
the peasant vassal of his day : “A kind of gloomy,
timid animal, emaciated, living in dens, eating
grass on all-fours, covered with rags, feeling
affirighted at the approach of other men, and yet
bearing the semblance of a human being, and yet
being a man.” This description will also apply to
the day-labourer of Europe. Miserably fed,
principally on potatoes and the refuse of the meat-
shops in the shape of sausages, poisoned with bad
liquors, which give him the deceptive sensation of
a satisfied appetite and renewed strength, badly
dressed in blouse and overalls which proclaim
him from afar as the poor man, the degraded
social being, condemned to physical uncleanliness
by his lack of money, he hides his wretchedness
in the darkest, filthiest corners of the great cities.
He not only has no share in the finer provisions
that the earth brings forth, but he is also partially
or totally deprived of light and a,ir which one
would suppose were at the disposal of every living
being in unlimited quantities. His insufficient
nourishment and the excessive demands upon his
labouring forces, exhaust his vital energies to such
an extent that his children are predisposed to
rachitis and he himself succumbs to an early
death, frequently preceded by some chronic
disease. His unhealthy dwelling place fastens
upon him and his offspring the curses of scrofula
and consumption. He is a kind of forlorn post
which every disease tries in turn to master. He
is worse off than the slave of ancient times;
oppressed the same, dependent in the same way
upon master and overseer, he yet gets nothing in
return for the loss of his freedom, not even the
food and shelter given to a domestic animal.
Another point in which his wretchedness is more
acute than that of the ancient bond-slave, is the
fact that he is conscious of it and also of his
dignity and natural rights as a man. He is even
worse off than the savage wandering through the
primeval forests of America or camping on the
grassy plains of Australia, for, like him, depen-
dent solely upon himself, like him, living from
hand to mouth, day by day, and suffering the
pangs of hunger if he lies idle fora few hours, he is,
unlike him, deprived of that keen delight which is
produced by the complete expansion of all the phy-
sical and mental forces in the struggle to overcome
natural obstacles, animals and men. He is more-
over obliged to pay over a considerable share of
his earnings, which are so far from being sufficient
for his support, to the community in exchange for
chains and blows. Civilisation, which promised
him liberty and prosperity, has not only refused
to keep its promise, but excludes him directly
from its highest blessings. Modern sanitary
science which has made the home of the rich so
comfortable and healthy, has not paid any atten-
tion to his lurking place. He is far more uncom-
fortable in the fourth-class coach when travelling
by rail than when he used to trudge along on foot
or ride behind some broken-down horse in his rude
cart. He never hears or knows anything of the
triumphs of scientific investigation. The produc-
tion of the creative arts, the poetical master-works
of his native tongue are sealed books to him,
because he has never been trained to comprehend
them. Even the labour-saving mechanical appli-
ances which ought to prove such a blessing to him,
have rather increased than diminished his slavery.
* * *
It is certainly a great forward stride in the pro-
gress and happiness of mankind that the forces of
nature can now be harnessed and employed in
the performance of all brute labour. What dis-
tinguishes man above all other living beings is not
his muscular system, but his brain. As a source
of strength he is inferior to the mule and the ox,
and if mechanical labour is all that is required of
him, he is degraded to be a mere beast of burden.
But machinery has not proved as yet the saviour,
the liberator and the ally of the workmen as was
first hoped, but on the contrary, has made him its
slave. _ Now as much as ever before, does his
value in the industrial arts depend directly upon
his muscular strength, and he has thus become
the weak, imperfect and abject competitor of
machinery. Deprived of his share of the soil, he
is not able to supply his wants by raising the pro-
ducts of nature ; submission to the inevitable is
his only resource. He only becomes aware of his
fellowship with mankind by the duties laid upon
him, for which he receives no privileges in return.
When he is not able to exchange his labour for
money, or when disease or old age put an end to
his work temporarily or permanently, the com-
munity looks after him, indeed. It gives him
alms if he takes to begging, it lays him on the cot
in the hospital if he has a fever, it puts him—
sometimes—in a poor-house, if he is too old and
feeble for anything else; but how impatiently,
how grudgingly, does it fulfill these duties ! It
offers its unwelcome guest more humiliations than
mouthfuls. While it is satisfying his hunger and
covering his nakedness, it is declaring that it is a
disgrace to accept these benefits from its hands
and affects the most profound contempt for the
unfortunates who are suing for its bounty. The
labouring classes find it impossible to lay by any-
thing for days of no work or of sickness and old
age. How can they have a surplus when even the
necessaries of life are lacking? They cannot
think of demanding wages above what they need
to satisfy their most pressing wants, because, as the
number of these disinherited beings is too large
and is constantly increasing, there are sure to be
plenty who would accept their situations at anv
wages that would keep them from dying at one*
of starvation. J s nce
* * *
These circumstances are utterly beyond the con-
trol of the labouring man. He may toil with the
utmost diligence, with the greatest exertion of his
vital energies, he can never earn more than is
sufficient to supply his most immediate wants—
aside from the fact that the lowest wages now
paid represent the expenditure of all the work-
man’s energies. On the contrary : the more he
works, the more intolerable does his position
become. This sounds paradoxical, but it is never-
theless true. The more that the operative
produces, the lower goes the selling-price of his
productions, while his wages remain the same, if
they do not become less. Thus he spoils his own
market by straining every nerve, and depreciates
the value of his own labour. This phe-
nomenon would not occur if the produc-
tion of the great manufacturing industries was
regulated by the demand. Then over-production
would never occur, the price of the articles would
never be depressed by an over-supply, and the
producing labouring man would be paid higher
wages for an increased amount of work. But
Capital perverts this natural operation of the
forces of political economy. A man builds a
factory and commences the manufacture of goods,
not because that he has become convinced that
a demand hitherto unsatisfied exists for the
goods he is to produce, but because he has
capital, for which he is seeking a profitable in-
vestment, and also because he has some neighbour
who has accumulated wealth with his factory.
Thus individual whims or want of judgment, in-
stead of the laws of political economy, decide the
investment of capital. The market is thus
flooded with an over-supply of certain manufac-
tured goods because some man has been following
a false trail in his mad chase after the Almighty
Dollar. The mistake brings its own punishment,
it is true. The manufacturer offers his goods at
lower and lower prices, until they no longer pay
the expenses of production, and then he is finan-
cially wrecked. All the other manufacturers of
that same article go down with him, and that
branch of production is involved in a national or
world-wide financial crisis. But the real victim is
the factory employe. As the price of the manu-
factured article sinks lower and lower, his wages
are decreased in proportion until the manufacturer
has exhausted his capital. And when the unequal
battle between supply and demand ends in the
victory of the forrn.er and production ceases, then
he is left entirely without bread, for a longer or
shorter time as the case may be. These are the
roles played by the manufacturer and the operative
in the great manufacturing industries. The latter
makes it possible for the former to accumulate
a great capital. This capital seeks profits and
believes they can be found in the opening of
additional factories. This leads to over-produc-
tion and increased competition, with their train of
depression of prices and reduction of wages, clos-
ing with the crisis which deprives the operative of
the opportunity of earning anything. Thus the
industrial slave makes tvs master rich, while his
own daily bread is reduced in quantity day by day
and finally taken away from him entirely.' Can
there be a more beautiful illustration of the way
in which the existing conditions of the economic
world conform to truth, justice and propriety !

country’s military system.

Mr. Eben Atherton, the well-known
Sydney medico, is dead. He steered a diplomatic
course between the homoeopathic cranks and the
other crowd, and made a fortune. He was a
man of strong medical instincts, and his death is
a loss to his profession.
The body of Robert Good, who served
as a trumpeter in the 6th Inniskillen Dragoons at
the Battle of Waterloo, and sounded the charge
which brought his regiment into conflict with the
French, lies buried in Concord cemetery, Sydney.
Good was long in the N.S.W. police.

A really touching farewell sermon was
preached by the new Bishop of Sydney in Birken-
head. His text was, “I will hope continually
and yet praise thee more and more. ” He referred
in a manly way to his departed wife, saying that
on the day of their engagement, 21 years ago, in
Birkenhead, they read that psalm together, and
the text would be cut on her tombstone.
Done to death by slanderous tongues
appears to have been the Rev. Wardrop, who
died last week in Sydney. He had the pluck to
stand up for Revs. Strong and Hods before the
Melbourne Presbytery, wherefore he got ousted
from a snug Geelong parsonage, and he has gone
to an untimely grave. A Wardrop cannot afford
to say in Australia what a Robertson-Smith, or
Principal Caird, writes with impunity in Scplatnd.
Pat Hanna, ex-Victorian M.P., died lately.
In old time he worked the ferry, at the wharf,
and he has often been referred to as a marvellous
self-made moneybags, but the fact is he was edu-
cated as an engineer. Once he was badly beaten
at an election, and made a characteristic speech,
which the local paper called "P. Hanna Out of
Tune.” He seemed very fond of the fun of sending
poor prisoners to quod from the City Police Court
bench.

perial sailors. When the additional ships of the
Australian squadron arrive this number will be
increased to 3400.

South Australia is suffering a “ recovery.”
The population is larger by 60,000 than it was ten
years ago, yet the spirit-duties have decreased by
£32,000. This is accounted for by most of the
professional temperance-lecturers being in gaol.
“I never assisted to cook a solicitor’s
accounts.” “I never put my mother in gaol. ’
“ I’ll tear out your windpipe.” “ You are a liar.”
“ If you were a Christian, you would be afraid of
being struck dead.”— ■N'.S.W. Legislative Assembly.
Henry George told a London audience
that South Australia leads the world with the
Single-tax. As that province only admits the
principle of a very small levy on unimproved
values, besides levying the income, stamp, and
revenue duties, we fail to see much Single-tax
about it.

If a man who says that those who speak of

fools,” why does Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan remain in
theN.S.W. Parliament? Hoes he consider the
present condition of society perfect? Is bis mind
utterly innocent of designs for improving the con-
dition of the people ?
“In the multitude of councillors there is
safety ” —such is the Bible motto engraved on the
tesselated pavement of the new vestibule at Mel-
bourne Parliament Houses. The meaning of this
aphorism, a grim joke of old Solomon’s, is not
generally understood. The safety is to the coun-
cillors, not to the counselled.
Says Senator Ingalls: “The purification
of politics is an iridescent dream. Government
is force. Politics is a battle for supremacy.
Parties are the armies. The decalogue and the
golden rule have no place in a political campaign.
The object is success. To defeat the antagonist
and expel the party in power is the purpose.”
The Trades and Labour Council of each Aus-
tralian province will, at next general election,
be the headquarters of Radical poll tics. The parlia-
mentary committee of the N.S.W. Council are
preparing a series of questions directly affecting
Labour to be put to candidates, who will be sup-
ported or opposed on the basis of their answers.
A respectable Sydney suburban tradesman
complains that he has been boycotted and ruined
through becoming a special constable. The
Bulletin is sorry to hear this, and can only
ascribe the man’s misfortune to the folly and
wickedness of the authorities in so arranging the
special-constabulary appointments as to set class
against class.
Wonderful and chameleonic are the
changes in Patterson, Victorian Customs Com-
missioner. This is the man who, under General
Berry (who now decorates the marble steps of the
Carlton Club or such places), declared that the
Government were going in for “broken heads and
flaming houses,” and yet Patterson would now
adorn the Toriest section of the Employers’
Union.
“ Ex-Diplomatist” tells how the Prince of
Wales converted Mundella, M.P., from a furious
Republican to a rank Tory. Mundella was to
address his constituents at Sheffield, but Wales
met him on the railway platform, introduced him
to Alexandra, and told him to tell the electors
they had pleasant memories of Sheffield. This
strangled the Republican, albeit he was the son of
an Italian carbonaro.
Watch the revolution in Belgium. The
dominant party is boldly named the Clerical, and
holds power only by resisting a fair suffrage. The
Clericals have 97 votes in the Chamber, against
the_ Opposition 41, but a suffrage on the French
basis, or even on that of England, would instantly
sweep the Clericals off the board. England virtu-
ally governs Belgium, and the English Conserva-
tives back up the reactionaries.
A telegram to the Australian daily press
states that when John Dillon, O’Brien and other
Ms.P. were charged at Tipperary last Thursday
with conspiracy to incite tenants not to pay rent
they refused to answer to their names, “ formally
protesting that the bench was unfairly composed. ”
Now, The Bulletin is ready to wager that what
Dillon and Co. said was not that the bench was
“unfairly composed,’’but that it was “packed”—
which in all human probability was the case.
“ Packed ” is too ugly a word for the cable-man.
The strike is evidently deranging the
Melbourne Aegus. “Really,” says she, “some
atmospheric or other mysterious cause seems to
be at work, compelling all classes to claim that
they have a right to be a law unto themselves.”
The italics (ours) show where the old lady is hurt.
She only wants the privileged classes to be a law
unto themselves. “Privilege,”in the Argus sense,
has got to die by the electricity of socialism.
Socialism, in its true sense, means the “ common
privilege of all classes ” —which is tantamount to
“no privilege,” for this is just the point in the
annulus where extremes meet.
“Labour always precedes wages. Paid
by the day, week or month, or by the piece, the
payment of wages by an employer always
implies the previous rendering of labour. As the
labourer who works for an employer does not get
his wages until he has performed work, his case
is similar to that of the depositor in the bank who
cannot draw money out until he has put money
in. During the time the labourer works for the
employer he is advancing capital to the employer,
but at no time, unless wages are paid before the
work is done, does the employer advance capital to
the labourer. ” —American paper.
Lady Dunlo is understood to aver that she
is quite good enough to be daughter-in law to the
cantankerous Earl of Clancarty, “ who is drinking
himself to death.” She adds that Lady Clancarty
had most to do with bringing the now celebrated
divorce-suit. “ You see she knows her husband
cannot live much longer at the rate he is now
going, and, of course, at his death, I would become
Countess Clancarty, while she would be put upon
the shelf as Dowager Countess. She is a tolerably
young woman yet, and don’t take kindly to that
idea at all, so she is trying to dispossess me of the
title.”
Colonel Price (Vic.), who told his soldiers
to “ fire low ” when they did fire at the strikers, is
a son of John Price (Maurice Frere, of Marcus
Clarke's “His Natural Life”), formerly Superin-
tendent of Convicts at Norfolk Island, and who
was killed at Pentridge by the prisoner Melville.
Blood will tell. Price explains, under pressure,
that while parading the militia from church ort a
Sunday morning he said : “ If you have to fire,
fire low, and lay them out, these disturbers of law
and order,” meaning thereby to hit ’em in the legs
(Irish fashion) and not hurt them overmuch. The
Bulletin cordially compliments Price, junior,
whose lamented father was never half so in-
genious on any recorded occasion. This inter-
pretation of a great mystery has but one modern
parallel—to be found in the history of a recent
Melbourne Stock Exchange scandal“ When I
said ‘ advance,’ I did not mean that the price was
to be advanced, but that we should advance to the
bar and take a drink.” Young man Price evi-
dently has not the nerve of old man Price, whe-
never condescended to explain or apologise t<
anybody.

THEN AND NOW.
Ere Blanky Dash was made a knight,
While still denied the “handle,”
He threw the money left and right—
The game was worth the candle.
But now, with Sir before his name,
His bounty fast decreases—
He’s broken up the former game,
And only keeps the pieces.
Good, Gracious Queen, we moui’n, alas !
The end of title-seeking,
A toady lavish with his brass
Is, in a way of speaking
Ear better than the stingy cur
Whose “ honour” ’s bought and paid for :
Then keep them waiting for the “Sir ”
Their golden traps are laid for.
* * *
if the Nellie Stewart v. Marian Burton
breach of agreement case ever comes into Court—
which we think is not at all likely—some interest-
ing glimpses of doings behind the curtain may be
expected. It is well-known that since the opening
night of “Paul Jones,” in Sydney, the Theatre
Royal has not been a hall of harmony—so far
from it, indeed, that sticking-plaster has been
called for at least on one occasion. If the case
does come into Court a big sensation looms ahead
for the evening press.
* * «
A well-known S.A. millionaire squatter
recently had a serious difference of opinion with
his only daughter, on account of her attachment
to his private secretary. The father objected to
her choice, but Juliet remained true to Romeo,
and, sooner than submit to the parental decision,
entered a private hospital as nurse. The old man,
however, has now given way, and a marriage will
take place shortly.
* * *
John Higgins, who died at the Royal
Hotel, Sydney, last week (in which house his
brother, Patrick Higgins, M.L.C., the well-known
Zigzag-railway-contractor, expired some years
ago), was a well-known figure in mining circles in
the N.S.W. metropolis, where he was generally
regarded as a rather crusty old bachelor. But it
was not so, for, ever so many years ago, when a very
young man, he married in Melbourne an amiable
girl “and was happy for a year,” as he sadly
said, glancing at the worn wedding-ring, which
was buried with him—“and then she died, and
I’ve been living a lonely life ever since at hotels
and clubs, and such places.” It will be remem-
bered by many that the death of Patrick Higgins
was in no small degree attributable to the loss of
his only son, a promising lad who, while travelling
in a coasting steamer, mysteriously disappeared
and was never again heard of.
* * * *
A sad event was the death at Paddington,
Sydney, last week, of Mrs. Annie Prince, who,
before taking a fatal dose of poison, wrote a
letter to her daughter stating that she was afraid
of becoming mad, and so had decided to prevent
herself being taken among,insane people. The
deceased lady was the widow of Mr. Prince, a
southern merchant, who years ago was a member
of the Melbourne Immigrants’ Home direc-
torate, which comprised the most beautifully
good-hearted and sympathetic set of men we
have ever seen banded together. And no more
need be said in praise of the lady who has just
died under such melancholy circumstances than
that she was possessed of a nature so gentle and
compassionate as to make her a fit wife for one
whose memory and that of his dead associates is
affectionately cherished by the poor.
# * *
Lord Kintore has been drivelling in his
long, bony way, to the effect that the descendant
of a long line of ancestors is necessarily careful to
prevent anything dimming the lustre of his
coronet. Kintore, being the son of a lay preacher
and a little in that line of business himself, is
doubtless ignorant of what goes on in the wicked
world, otherwise he might have called to mind
that England’s premier Earl, viz., Shrewsbury,
eloped with a married woman, that the Marquis
of Ailesbury is the companion of blacklegs, that
the Duke of Marlborough used to kick his wife,
and that the late Lord Aylesford was a notorious
blackguard, and that a hundred other heads of
“families ” nearly as old, or older than, Kintore’s
have been good enough to show reason why all the
coronets in Christendom should be carted off to
the pawnshop for the benefit of the poor, and the
tickets burnt by the common hangman.
# # *
While Zola, whom Pastor Bevan sets
down as almost moral, from his excellent inten"
tions, is ruthlessly vetoed by the Victorian
Customs, Theophile Gautier’s audacious Mdlle. de
Maupin, American-English translation, is freely
sold at the Government Railway Bookstalls. Anri
yet it is “frankly and unblushingly immoral.”
Surely, if Nana be an emanation from the sixth
hell, Mdlle. de Maupin is from the seventh. If
we were asked now to pick out a wicked book for
the Auger Society .

Some ghastly stories are current concern-
ing the sufferings endured at sea by the captains
of non - unionist - manned Australian coasting-
steamers.
* * *
Everybody is supposed to know the law,
but a judge doesn’t know it till a barrister tells
him, and the barrister, as a rule, is equally ignorant
till a solicitor enlightens him. If the judge knew
the law, then the barrister would be unnecessary;
and, if the barrister knew it, then the solicitor
would be superfluous. As a rule, however, they
are all three superfluous. The only individual
who is not superfluous is the client.
* * *
For reliable information concerning female
character a fairly intelligent benedict can give
the shrewdest of bachelors ninety-nine points in
one hundred. Although no husband satisfactorily
fathoms the mysteries of his own wife’s character,
the deep insight he obtains into those of other

* * *
Margaret Elizabeth, Countess of Jersey,
must be a genius—that is, for a titled person ; for
we are told that “ her oratorical gifts are equal to
those of any woman in England”—a tough state-
ment when we remember that Annie Besant is
still alive and well. Then, again, Lady Jersey is
“a bright star of the Primrose League, but has
failed to incur the enmity of its opponents.” The
Australian Leagues have long languished on the
verge of dissolution, and badly want her aid along
“the primrose path that leads unto the everlasting
bonfire.”
* * #
The friends of the Mount Rennie convicts
have sent in a petition to Governor Carington,
asking him to extend mercy to the survivors ere
he leaves Sydney. The Bulletin now tells
Governor Carington that the Mount Rennie busi-
ness (in the words of the late Mr. Dailey, Q.C.)
“amounted to a rape on the Goddess of Justice ;
it affixed the brand of Cain to the forehead of
New South Wales.”
# * *
A bigamist at last Tamworth (N.S.W.)
Assizes pleaded “ Not Guilty,” as he did not con-
sider his first marriage binding, in that he was
“ blind-drunk ”at the time. The jury, however,
found that the knot also was tight.

received printed orders to “aim loic” in the
event of their being required to “ shoot the devils
down.”
LOW AIMS.
(Foe Soldiees Only.)
Aim low! Quite so
When rulers try
That game their aim
Is never high.
Aim low, so low !
Should any die,
Can’t blame your aim—
It wasn’t high.
Aim tow? No, no;
You wonder why ?
Low aim, sad shame —
Much better high.
Aim low, when foe
Is prancing nigh
On horse ? Of course
Aim rather high.
Aim high, let fly
At Capting Blow,
Then sob, “Bad job—
Through aiming low.”

Oneperson at the table besides the Emperor is singu-
iarly honoured, and that is Mr. Gibson Miller, who is an
Australian gentleman devoted to yachting. He is about
to establish a club in the Antipodes, and bou-dit the
Duke of Norfolk’s yacht, now known as Saidde. His name
came up for election at the suggestion of the Prince of
Wales, and he was elected by acclamation—a mark of
exceptional and probably unprecedented favour. Prior
to the election Mr. Miller had left our shores; but on
hearing the glad tidings he immediately posted back to
England, and arrived to-day just in time for the brilliant
gathering of to-night.
Bliss!

They are probably interesting and delicate
“ family reasons ” which induces© many Victorian
members of Parliament to take such a deep interest
in the welfare of the aborigines and their half-
caste offspring at the Framlingham Reserve. The
biography of one Victorian “benefactor ” tells the
story of a man who loved the Australian gin both
wisely and well, and proved an indulgent father
to his half-breed picaninnies, but who, dying,
bequeathed his ample fortune mostly to “ white ”
charities, leaving his semi-coloured heirs to the
official mercy of the State. May the Lord have
official mercy on his soul. Amen.
* * *
Mr. Irving Winter-Irving, a Victorian
land-owner, who lately enlarged his surname by
Royal patent, or something, has belched forth
his sentiments concerning the strike. He thinks,
does Irving Winter-Irving, that “ a little starva-
tion would not do unionists much harm.” Down
Bendigo way they seem to have been offering up
groans for I. W. 1., which was both rude and
futile behaviour. Let us rather sing to him :
0, Irving Winter-Irving,
Wouldn’t starving do you good?
You have many servants serving
You with many sorts of food.
If you didn’t eat so often,
Would a gnawing pain inside
Have a tendency to soften
Down your moneybaggy pride ?
0, Irving Winter-Irving,
Letters-patent from the Crown
May have set your lip a curving
With a sense of wide renown :
When your name was Irving Winter
And the hyphen wasn’t thefe,
Were you then, at heart, a stinter
Of the humble toilers’ fare ?
O, Irving Winter-Irving,
Of no union are you
A member staunch, unswerving ?
Are you not a Tory blue ?
You are, you are—you know it:
You belong to the &ite,
And your class (opines the poet)
Gets a sinful lot to eat.
0, Irving Winter-Irving,
Thou pompous pile of pelf !
Every working man’s deserving
Of more victuals than yourself;
Each land-“ lord ” is a fetter
Holding industry in thrall,
And the world would go on better
If starvation killed you all.
# # *
While the strike accentuates the bitterness
of Melbourne poverty, the high-toned State school-
masters rigorously keep out all children without
shoes or stockings. In fact, the free State schools
are only tony places for the children of those able
to pay, and such must not mix with the little
gutter-snipes.
* * *
Admiration is claimed, in Melbourne, for
one of those familiar mutual-admiration groups of
testimonial photos. It is divided into Esquires,
Misters, and plain men not even Mistered, quite
like orders of nobility, the principle being that
which governed old black Sam Johnson : “ When
I gib £1 to de church, de parson called me Samuel
Johnsing, Esquiah. When I gib 10s., I was Mr.
Johnsing. When I gib 55., I cum down to
Brudder Johnsing, and now I gib only a shillun,
I’m dat ole nigga Johnsing.”
* * *
On behalf of Cuddy, the embezzling
manager of a Melbourne Financial Company, a
clergyman stated that he had known prisoner for
ten years, during which time he had been treasurer
of a church, etc. This proves nothing, except
that the parson was too smart to give Cuddy a
chance of sneaking the pew-rents. Perhaps,
though, it was intended as a plea for mercy, on
the ground that the man’s honesty had been neces-
sarily undermined by professional religion.
* * *
An English dentist has paid £5 damages
for the privilege of pulling out the wrong tooth by
mistake.
* * *
Smellbourne papers have been regaling
their readers with whole pages of “Mr. Mansergh’s
Report on Sewage Disposal.” This light, spark-
ling literature happens to possess special charms
j ust now for the nobility and gentry of Toorak,
who are considerably down with fresh typhoid
from the cow.
* * *
A non-unionist bishop was humping cargo
lately at Geraldton (W.A.). This is the first hard
work the Church has done since it used to knock-
out human brains in the days of the Crusades.
# # *
Melbourne tradesmen have been hanging
out coats-of-arms of various designs, each purport-
ing to be the genuine Hopetoun label. Someone
has described the real article in the local Hekald,
setting forth that two ladies in loose garments and
a broken globe under a rainbow constitute the
Earl’s badge. Thanks.
# * *
A baby boy belonging to an Otago man
has been christened “ Sir Robert Stout M‘Lavish.”
This opens up an almost original field for whole-
sale toadyism, but has the merit of making titles
common property and bringing them within the
reach of the poorest. A man whose name was
Major Ooakley got credit in New Zealand for
thousands of pounds simply because he was sup-
posed to be a retired major. When he burst up,
however, it was discovered that Major was simply
his front name.

1 * * *
Within five months there have been before
the English and Scotch Courts no less than seven
eases in which servants have opened their mis-
tresses’ confidential letters, by means of holding
the envelopes over a boiling kettle and relaxing
the gum. The post-official, however, does not
work his little game by aid of steam ; all he wants
is a blunt-edged pen-holder. Moral: It is time to
revert to sealing-wax.
The Bulletin notices, with much pain,
the hungry appearance of the amateur mounted
policemen and their horses now in the service of
theN.S.W. Government. The remedy for this,
so far as the men are concerned, is More Pie.
* * •
Gov. Kintore is classed as a first-rate shot,
and can bring his birds down with right and left
barrel as quick as you can say “Sir William
Robinson.” His only other apparent speciality is
sitting in the racecourse grand-stand smoking
strong cigars in the presence of the ladies. But,
then, the Prince of Wales does that.

Thirteen is the Devil’s number. The
Bulletin advises Gov. Oarington to attend no
more than 12 farewell banquets.
It is said that the Sydney men who bought
Thompson and Giles’ stock (sold by that firm
owing to the resumption of their premises by
Government) have already cleared £lO,OOO by their
bargain, and expect to nett £15,000.
• * *
People are hereby cautioned against rush-
ing madly to Peak-hill. The reefs have been
collared by a N.S.W. parliamentary syndicate in
utter defiance of every principle which should
regulate dealings between the Grown and the
bona-fide working-miner, and the place is being
boomed by the Press in the interests of certain
land-sharks who, months ago, acquired possession
of the chief business sites, and who have—er—
strong representation among certain newspaper-
proprietaries.
Four men were being tried for theft re-
cently in France, when the judge declared the
proceedings up to date to be null and void, because
one of the jurymen was asleep. There are various
kinds of judges. Some of those that grow in
Australia would have gone right on and hanged
the defendants though eleven jurymen were asleep
and the twelfth one was dead.
* » *
A man who recently had a law-suit against
a certain Irish-Australian politician went into
court and saw eleven Irishmen on the jury, ten of
them from Tipperary—his friend’s county. “ Your
Honor,” said the disgusted plaintiff, “ I’ll pay all
costs if you’ll postpone the case till next sittings,”
Postponement granted and meanwhile plaintiff
will see whether he can’t round up a jury.
# * #
Four months ago, at Redfern, Sydney, a
boy was sent by his sister to get some white sand.
He never came”back, and last week his body was
discovered beneath the sand. This recalls a grisly
occurrence of old Sydney. Many years ago a
young fellow and his sweetheart suddenly dis-
appeared. They had gone out one evening together
and were heard of no more until their skeletons
were discovered in a local sandhill, their arms
around one another. They had sat beneath
the hill, a large mass of which had suddenly f»Uen
and suffocated them,
» * *
Lady Hopetoun’s announced return to
England “for her health” comes as a surprise.
By the way, it’s always a big mistake for a man
to bring his wife’s relations with him to Australia.

Adelaide pressmen are very properly com-
plaining of the political patronage shown in
connection with secretaryships and tne shorthand-
reporting of select committees. These appoint-
ments are now conferred upon ex-members of the
S.A. Assembly, who, in most cases, are inferior
hands, and, moreover, take the billets “on the
cheap,” thus sweating out the pressmen.
# « *
The smash of the Cape of Good Hope
Bank is a most serious affair, and looks like the
Premier Permanent and the Glasgow Bank affairs
rolled into one, for the Cape is flourishing. There
are eleven banks, but the one that has broken does
about half of all the local financial business. A
million has gone, and now they are looking for
the woman.
Roundabout.
Bear Bulletin :—One of the worst aspects
of the strike, and one which you seem to have
entirely overlooked, is that everybody insists on
talking about it. It pervades the streets, and the
churches and the newspapers and the hotels, and
it lifts up its voice in the hash-house—in short it
is everywhere. I am writing this in an upper
room, hut down below the rest of the company
are yelling at each other over the same old topic,
and they will probably continue to yell till far
into the night. The old person with the asthma,
and the deadly wheeze in his windpipe, and the
1141 other symptoms is screaming in a vain effort
to make himself heard above the din ; and the
imported softgoods individual from Belfast, whose
one fixed idea is that the Pope habitually goes
around with dynamite and wants to blow things
up, is clamouring amid a general riot and carnage
of idiots in the attempt to demonstrate that the
strike is in some way owing to the pernicious
influence of Rome. Then there is a red headed
person who balances his food on his knife, and
looks thoughtfully at it, and then shoves the
whole implement down his throat, and he thinks
that the whole disturbance is an underhand device
to bring down the price of varnish because he
trades in that article; and another gentleman,
who is partially red-headed and partially bald,
can be indistinctly heard above the roar and bang
of the debate, imploring the others to keep quiet
till he speaks ; and another indignant item who is
absolutely hairless is exploding with fury because
he has been talking in a corner at the very top of
his voice for two hours, and nobody has even
looked at him. Each one of the five makes a
row like the combined note of the eleven devils
that continually howl round Judas Iscariot, and
they all shriek jointly and simultaneously, and
every one is convinced that he could be heard a
Seat d,eal more distinctly if the other four would
ive off. But then—alas ! —they never do leave
off, and you can never comprehend the full horror
of a strike until you have heard it discussed in all

A pair of Sydney detectives went forth one
evening last week to collect evidence against a hair-
dresser who was supposed to have a totalisator
concealed somewhere about his premises. They
disguised themselves before starting, that is to say,
they put on each other’s boots, and brushed their
hair straight up, and assumed that air of fathom-
less mystery which the detective always wears
when he has forgotten his own name and address ;
and then they walked into the suspected house
arm in arm, throwing out their legs at precisely
the same angle, and sat down in two chairs to
wait for evidence. Neither of them offered any
observation; they merely planted themselves and
glared hungrily like two spiders waiting for a fly.
The barber recognised them, and flew round
excitedly and asked them if they would have their
hair cut, but they replied hoarsely that they
didn’t require any assistance. Next moment a
hairless youth slouched in and began to state that
he would invest half-a-sovereign Just here,
however, the barber rushed him backwards into
the chair, and lathered him, and the sentence was
left unfinished. He struggled wildly to escape,
and swore fearfully, and kicked out like a fury,
but the barber whispered fiercely in his ear that if
he said a word about horses he would strangle him
on the spot and bury his remains in-—— but at
this moment a long, knock-kneed larrikin put his
head through the doorway to inquire what odds
he could get about a steeplechase. He didn’t get
far enough to commit himself, however, for with
one jump the other assistant seized him and
began to cut his hair, and told him in a
muffled undertone that if he attempted to say a
word about steeplechases he would never live to
finish the sentence. Then, with a trembling
hand, he dragged out some of his hair by the
roots, cut off a piece of his ear with the scissors,
and threw him out. He went away thoughtfully
and had several drinks, and then, growing partly
oblivious, he came back to renew his question
about the steeplechase, but the instant his head
appeared in the doorway he was partially
strangled and his hair cut a second time. He
retired in a bald condition and greatly surprised ;
and the detectives made an entry to the effect that
no suspicious circumstances had so far come under
their notice. A huge wharf-labourer burst through
the doorway a moment later, and started to
announce that he would invest five bob on
both the assistants hurled themselves on him and
shampooed him at this moment, while he shrieked
for the police and discharged a fiery blast of
language at them, but he was overcome and led
out-in a fearful state of indignation. There was
five minutes’ calm after this, till a stalwart young
woman arrived with a message from her brother,
and she came so near to giving the whole business
away that she was partially shaved in the excite-
ment, and had her hair singed and her fringe
completely demolished, but at last she broke away
and went into hysterics outside. Three
minutes later her father came round to wreck
the whole shop, and one of the assistants
was assaulted before they successfully crushed the
old man into a seat and oiled his head in the midst
of his ravings. It was evident by this time that a
crisis was at hand, for it required the whole avail-
able force on the premises to hold this last
infuriated customer down and put some hair-
restorer on him, and if anyone else came in and
tried to speak it would be impossible to stifle him
without letting the old man out of the chair, and
if once he got out it was certain that he would kill
somebody. But just as the barber and his
employe had resolved to let go, and run for their
lives, and leave all the stock, fixtures, fittings and
utensils behind them, the two officers made an
entry that none but a legitimate trade was evi-
dently conducted on the premises, and then they
rose together and clumped heavily .away. When
they were gone, matters were explained to the
apoplectic father in the chair, and business gra-
dually assumed its normal aspect, except that the
big wharf-labourer has taken out a summons for
shampooing under arms accompanied with vio-
lence, and refuses to be pacified.
* * *
For plain and fancy stupidity the private
detective is supreme, and an Adelaide (S.A.) lady
who lately hired one of them has just realised th’s
fact. Her household consisted of herself and
husband, together with her father who had
arrived from the country on a visit; and she was
a prey to dark suspicions which rendered her life
a misery. There were unaccountable hairs on the
husband’s coat for one thing, and strange patches
of hair-oil sometimes arose on his shirt-front, and
he seemed to forget who she was at times and
called her by a wide variety of names, and then
she would smile upon him while she inwardly
longed to throw the tongs at his villainous head.
And at last her hidden misery becoming too great
for endurance she hired a badly-disguised ex-
policeman who was supposed to be full of deep
sagacity and research, and put him on the trail at
a salary of £4 per week. He was a lumbering,
fatuous, heavy-footed person with a wooden ex-
pression, and had gained his reputation because
he could arrest a bigger larrikin single-handed
than any other man in the city, but he cheerfully
undertook to find out everything under the sun.
And he did it. He traced the offender to bars
and billiard-rooms, and gazed through the window
at him while he hugged a barmaid and asked her
to fly with him, and he hovered behind him as he
threw bouquets to the painted angels of the
ballet. He was trodden on while glaring through
keyholes, and fallen over while listening on stair-
cases, and he nearly got into trouble through
assisting his victim home and searching his
pockets as he went. He reported all these things
regularly, and drew his salary and expenses punc-
tually, and then he would go forth refreshed, and
hide himself under a table somewhere to_ listen to
something, and somebody would kick him under
the impression that he was a dog. He charged
extra for being kicked, and when he was trodden-
on in the execution of his duty the bill used to
mount up alarmingly. It took eleven weeks and
a total outlay of £l2O to finish the investigation,
and when his labours were completed it accidentally
transpired that he had been following his em-
ployer’s father instead of her husband all the time,
and the whole mass of harrowing evidence referred
to the wrong man. Next day an outraged daughter
led the most crestfallen parent in the country to
the railway-station and bade him go home and
stay there for the rest of his life under pain of
exposure and instant vengeance. There are still
mysterious hairs on the husband’s coat, and
patches of hair-oil on his shirt-front.
Lily Bagley, of Melbourne, was tried the
other day for shooting with a liberal and promis-
cuous recklessness at the man who seduced her,
and was duly acquitted. The jury probably
wanted to bring in a verdict of “Served him
right,” but the lamentable barrenness of the
judicial system blocked this expression of opinion.
The man was only slightly injured, but then very
few women ever can shoot straight enough to
bring about a funeral.
Mr. Stanhope, the Tory Secretary of War,
recently offered an entirely new theory in defence
of that fallen ruin with the umbrella, Field-
Marshal Gamp. “A man who had been fifty
years in the service of his country,” he wailed,
“must necessarily have greater authority than
any man, however able or eminent, who succeeds
him.” What an amount of authority George 111.
must have accumulated in his latter days of weep-
ing idiocy, when his sons used to tie him to the
bedpost and jab pins into him by way of light
recreation i
Parkes’s apology to M‘Millan is a painful
evidence that the old man is going downhill. In
the days when he was still strong and vigorous
Parkes never apologised. He offered an explana-
tion now and then, but nothing more, and when
he had finished it he generally went out to the
back and threw away the ear of the person ex-
plained to. If the explanation was long and
intricate lie not unfrequently had his mouth full
of the other party’s whiskers and hair towards the
end of the discussion, and he seldom failed to
make matters clear before he was done. And if
any man came round then to ask Sir ’Enry what
he meant by his offensive language, a voice could
be heard all over the street singing out in a pen-
sive tune such expressions as “withered tarantula!”
“ go out and bark with the dogs!” and the like.
Consequently, when it became known that
M'Millan was in search of information, most of
the older politicians fully expected that he would
come back hamstrung and with all his money
borrowed off him, and some even feared that he
might find it hard to escape with his clothes.
But. alas, the old Parkes wasn’t there, and the
new Parkes actually sorter apologised.
Evidence.
Ask any successful lawyer to what he ascribes
his success, and he will tell you that he has taken
care to have had “ evidence ” to support the cases
he had undertaken. What sends the murderer to
the gallows ? Evidence. What sends the thief to
gaol ? Evidence. What procures a divorce from
a cruel husband ? Evidence. Facts can only be
proved by evidence, and that is how the superiority
of Clements Tonic is proved by the pure and
positive testimony of its various patrons, over all
its imitators, for Clements Tonic has no compeers
or competitors. It is now quite unnecessary for
the manufacturers of Clements Tonic to say a
word in its favor, its reliability, virtues, merits
and therapeutical efficacy are proved beyond all
doubt by the unanimous verdict, |the irrefutable,
incontrovertible, and indisputable evidence of its
influential patrons.
T. B. Bryce, Esq., J.P., writing in the Man-
ning Riveh Times says, “We personally know
some of the sufferers who have taken Clements
Tonic and derived wonderful relief from it.”
W. C. McDougall, Esq., writing in the Bal-
main Observer says, “ The good effects of
Clements Tonic have come under our notice both
by personal experience and by that of two residents
of Balmain.”
Spenser Groves, Esq., writing in the Tumut and
Adelong Times, says, “We have had proof
positive here, in this neighbourhood of the wonder-
ful properties of Clements Tonic.”
W. B. Howarth, Esq., writing in the Grenfell
Record, says, “We have found it of great value
for the hot weather and have had personal experi-
ence, so has a friend of ours in the civil service
who was so sick that he threw up everything but
his appointment, but Clements Tonic cured him.”
S. Beach, Esq., Marrickville, (N.S.W.), says,
“ That Clements Tonic did wonders for Miss
Beach, that four doses only cured both toothache
and earache. *
F. Bagnall, Esq., Ravenhurst, Marrickville,
says, “Mrs. Bagnall took Clements Tonic with
the most gratifying result, and that its use re-
sulted in a restoration of perfect health after two
years of extreme debility. ”
John Plnmmer, Esq., North Willoughby, says,
“ Clements Tonic did Mrs. Plummer a great deal
of good and it is a very good remedy.”
J. W. L. Kyle, Esq., postmaster, Manilla, says,
“ Before I had used one bottle of Clements Tonic
I was quite cured.”
Captain Dibden, Balmain, says, “ That four
bottles completely cured him of debility and
nervousness, and he is glad to bear witness to the
value of such a wonderful remedy. ”
No one ever hears evidence like that, however,
of any of the harmful nostrums sold in so many
shops in place of the genuine Clements Tonic, for
although they copy our advertisements, &c., with-
out stmt, they cannot copy us in our proofs
because such proof is not procurable. A medicine
must have merit before people will lend their
praise and signatures in support of it. Persevere
with the remedy. It must cure, if you only take
enough of it. Remember that in building a house
it has to be done stone by stone, brick by brick,
slate by slate and so it is with disease, you can
only cure by persistent treatment, you can’t blow
weakness out of the system by dynamite, you
must restore lost energy by natural and continued
treatment. Insist on Clements, it is sold every-
where.
