The Referee.
The widow of the Hon. .Tames White has
Redded not to break up the Kirkham stud.
« * *
The Australian Club make a mistake in putting
ihamois-leather gloves on the light spatters. Half the
battle from an onlooker’s point of view, in a show set-to
lies in’the clean hitting and loud-sounding slaps from the
kid gloves.
# * *
Notwithstanding the antagonism of the big
Jockey Clubs, pony-raoing flourishes apace about Sydney.
The biggest meeting yet held is now in course at Moore-
geld when, amongst other big money, there are two
races of £l5O each. Captain “ Tom ” Merry took back to
America a glowing account of our pony-racing, and one
of the principal ’Frisco papers has taken the matter up,
advocating the formation of pony and galloway clubs,
observing that they will take a great anxiety from the
shoulders of big breeders who have hitherto not had any
market for their undersized stock.
* * *
Occasionally the horse-training reporter graces
his copy with a new flower of speech culled from the
squalid back-yard of his blighted brain. He gets tired of
saying that something strode along freely, or did long,
strong work, or indulged in healthy exercise, &c. A '
Flemington tout announces that “ Lady Agnes was noted
mmtina smartly,” which is intended to explain that the
mare had a fast canter around the ordinary track. It
doesn’t mean that Lady Agnes was waltzing.
* #
One bookmaker had £lBOO staked on the South
Melbourne and Carlton football-match !
# # *
The Fitzroy (Vic.) footballers have got an ex-
pensive opinion from a barrister about a certain goal
which they claim to have kicked in a late match with
Port Melbourne. They are going to get another powerful
opinion from another barrister, and if it is also favourable,
they intend to go into the Supreme Court and recover
that goal and costs, if it takes all the money they have to
tio it. The spectacle of six judges wrestling with a ques-
tion about a goal, not a gaol, is a grim commentary on
the degraded stupidity of Australia.
* * *
The Australian Eleven have not done so badly
in their tour as would appear to an outsider. There is no
doubt they have played with very hard luck, as most of
the drawn matches were much in their favour. No
previous Australian Eleven has had to meet such strong
elevens, and the estimation in which the Britishers held
them may be gleaned from the fact that Peel, Lehmann,
Briggs and Attewell chased them ail over the United
Kingdom. And—no doubt a delicate subject to touch
upon—it is quite surprising the number of Australian
wickets lost by the umpires’ decisions of “leg before.”
* * *
Gresford has been purchased by Mr. “Bravo”
Jones for £lOOO, and is a cheap colt at the money. He is
engaged in the Cup at 7 stone.
« * *
“ The smaller the men the quicker the fighting,”
and Willis and Holden, at the Sydney Amateur Gymnastic
Club, on the 24th inst., were no exception to the rule.
T,he contest was for the “bantam” championship, and
blood and hair fairly flew till the middle of the second
round, when Holden was counted out. It is on the cards
that Willis will go to America.
# * *
Slavin’s defeat of M'Auliffe came rather as a
pleasurable surprise, for previous to Slavin’s departure
from Australia, he had, though a most promising man,
never shown form entitling him to be considered a tip-
topper. He defeated Farnan, Peter Jackson's conqueror,
but Tom Lees also beat Farnan twice, and when Jackson
went under he was a stripling, and lucky when he got a
square meal. Slavin also defeated the Yankee, Costello,
and put Mick Dooley through his facings twice, but it
must be confessed that at one portion of Dooley’s first
fight it was a toss up who won. Taking a line from that
most uncertain guide, the time occupied in defeating a
man, Slavin should be on velvet in meeting Jackson, as it
took Peter 24 rounds to do what Slavin got through in
less than two. Slavin may not get on a match with
Sullivan easily, as he appears to be more of the showman
than the boxer now. Slavin is undoubtedly a bom
fighter, and game as vou make them. He was obviously
designed by Nature to take punishment and to alter the
shape of men’s countenances. One of the most awe-
inspiring of sights is to see Mr. Slavin, with his hair
cropped short and some blood on his forehead, glare and
rush at the fellow who has just twice punched him
hard in the mouth.
* # *
“ Bowler ” Ferris, having been provided with a
bank billet, will, imitating Spoff. and remaining in
England, join the Gloucester County Eleven.
# * *
Rowley Pickering, sporting editor of Sydney
Mail, said the other day that he and Elias Laycook had
shot 666 gill-birds in one day. He was informed that 666
was the number of the Beast, and that he (It. P.) was
therefore Antichrist or something. Scripture will prove
anything.
m * *
Positively the unluckiest racing-man in Aus-
tralia is Sir W. J. Clarke, Bart. However, it is well that
those who have all the money don’t enjoy all the luck.
His Claret, a horse, won the Caulfield Steeplechase the
other day. Turfites oast up their logs for another win by
the Bart, from time immemorial, or since his antediluvian
mare Petrea. He must have given the bookmakers a
whole station.
* * *
Dooley has signed articles to meet .Toe Goddard
in a match to a finish on November 1, in the Melbourne
Athletic Club rooms.
* # *
Jackson and Go'ddard meet in an eight-round
tussle for £4OO, of which sum the winner “cops” £3OO,
the loser £lOO, on October 20 at the Crystal Palace Hall,
Richmond (Melb.) Murphy, the defeated, guarantees to
knock Griffo, his conkeror, out in eight rounds one week
after the big ’uns have settled their hashing business.
# ■» *
Charley Dunn, who was defeated by Fogarty in
less than two rounds, wants the Australian Club to “ put
up” £l5O for him to fight Jim Ryan or Jack Molloy; and
Fogarty wants to meet Ryan for a £5OO purse.
# * *
There was a big row at the Sydney Driving Park
meeting on Saturday, when Fairy Boy started for the sell
ing race at 6 to 4 on, and lazily ran into third place. Th(
public yelled for gore and were not mollified until his
owner was “sent up ” for life, and the jockey for twelve
months. Ponies are good property just now, and Fairy
Boy was claimed by Dr. Slate for £lOO.
# ‘ * *
English cricketer Shrewsbury does not think it
would pay to tour the colonies in ’92. The writer differs
from Shrewsbury. If a team were brought out whose
motto was “ put on the runs as fast as you can for fear it
should come on to rain,” then the trip would be no failure.
* # *
What has become of the money subscribed for
the Searle memorial ? The movement is as dead as poor
Searle himself.
* # «
The Sydney totalisator and consultation men
are being prosecuted, obviously in the interest of the
Sydney bookmakers, who are allowed to break the law all
the time.
» * *
The two elevens of female English cricketers
who starred Britain last season are expected to visit
Australia. If not more profitably engaged, George
Bonnor would make a splendid coach and manager of
lady cricketers.
* # •
It is said that Neilson rowed his mile in the
Matterson race in smin. 82secs. with a head wind against
him, but as he failed to go much farther it can scarcely be
deemed a record as part of a row over the full course.
« « #
Choynski, who meets Fogarty soon, has only
been a few days in Sydney. During that time Barron has
knocked out Saxon in the first round, Willis has treated
Holden similarly in the second, and Fogarty has also sent
Dunn to sleep in the second round, and the American
athlete in three “ big ” fights has only seen two rounds
completed.
* # *
The Irish amateur cyclist, M'Credy, has won
the 50-miles safety bicycle championship in record time of
2hr. 29min. 55 l-ssec., or at the rate of 20 miles an hour
throughout. Much of the late success in record-breaking
seems to be due to the use of pneumatic tyres, whose
benefits in racing some of the Sydney know-alls pooh-
poohed so liberally a short time ago.
THE VERY BEST
A single pain is often the first indication, and
the last throe, in one, of a physical climax, and
the climax is death. That St. Jacobs oil has
again and again wrought its miracles just in the
nick of time, has bridged over the critical emerg-
ency, and has
SAVED MANY LIVES
at the point of death, is fully attested by the
record of many extraordinary cures in which this
fact of saving life is duly set forth.
If to its remarkable virtues in the permanent
cure of chronic cases of Rheumatism, Neuralgia,
Lumbago, Sciatica, after years of endurance,
wherein everything else proved valueless, fortunes
were spent in vain, and crutches and crippled
limbs were the manifestations, is to bo added its
supreme sovereignty over pain, even unto death,
i it is whollv entitled to the appellation of
THE VERY BEST.
Its specific action is especially adapted with a
| curative penetration and soothing effect, to arrest
any pain at the acme of its worst influence. St.
: Jacobs oil applied externally is now known as the
! best specific to arrest the fatal tendency of any
! aggravated pain.
j A MOST REMARKABLE CASE
j can be cited in proof, which is thoroughly verified,
j It is in its main features taken from a leading
1 Liverpool journal as follows: “Two years ago,^
| states Mr. William Buchanan, residing at No. 8,
! St. John’s-road, Kirkdale, Liverpool, and twenty-
I four years in the service of the Cunard Steamship
1 Company, “I was taken down with a most
| excruciating pain in my head, winch was followed
’ by
ONE YEAR OF TORTURE.
All physicians believed that it would lead to
softening of the brain and death. Six of the
most eminent attended me ; but gave no relief. X
had to be held down, and my screams were heard
outside. My case was deemed incurable. My
wife procured a bottle of St. Jacobs oil, and
applied it to my head. It acted like magic. I
was cured by it, and am now in perfect health,
and I have not had the slightest return since I
was cured some months ago. ” Nothing more need
be added.
A NUGGET OF WISDOM.
GEORGE ELIOT'S OPINION OF SWISS
WATCHES.
“These little Geneva toys are cleverly con-
structed to go always a little wrong. But if you
wind them up and set them regularly every night
you may know at least that it’s not noon when tbs
hands point there.’’ — FROM “Felix Holt the
Radical,” Chap. XXII.
NOTE. —We are indebted to our East Indian
correspondent (M. A. J. Faulding), for this quo-
tation from a popular novelist, whose works are
often referred to as containing “Nuggets of
wisdom,” and whose opinion regarding cheap
Swiss watches was delivered long years before the
Waterbury watches were ever dreamed of. The
difference between a “Geneva toy” and a reliable
and accurate Waterbury has been amply demon-
strated. The Waterbury is a timekeeping watch
in the best sense of the word. — Exchange.
CAUTION.
If you want correct time and a watch which, in
event of accident, can be cheaply and efficiently
repaired, AVOID the cheap, trashy. Swiss burglar
baits sold to the unwary as timekeepers. Consult
public opinion in the matter —not the interests of
some pettifogging dealer, who only too eagerly
foists upon you a useless, rubbishy, and worthless
article, for the sake of gaining extra profit, and
who cares little for your execrations which will
> surely be showered on his head in the near future,
xx you consult public opinion you will beyond a
shadow of a doubt BUY A WATERBURY,
the testimony in favour of which as a reliable and
accurate timekeeper is overwhelming; and which,
moreover, in event of injury, can be efficient-
ly repaired at low cost by taking or sending
it to any of the company’s repair agencies,
established in various centres of the colonies.
All Watches Manufactured by the Waterbury
Watch Co. have the word “ WATERBURY"
PRINTED in fine type upon their dial faces,
AND ALSO THE INTERWOVEN LETTERS W.W.Co.,
WITHOUT WHICH NO WATCH SOLD AS A WATER-
BURY IS GENUINE.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor unit be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under : —
t. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. (Drawings must be made in dean lines
gf Indian ink on white paper or Bridol-board.)
5. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
3, Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred).
h. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
6. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. The
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, aoid not merely on an accompany■
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.

THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literary
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
sistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces—
Religious Interference in Politics,
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
Influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
firs? issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
'.ocal and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form-printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudai
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress : it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege—the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligeut bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more them a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official —that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
ether paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and beat, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

Belinda’s Smile.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Of all the girls I ever knew,
Belinda Banks was boss;
A pious, wintry chill she threw
On all she came across.
Her friends would rather run a mile
Than stop to face Belinda’s smile.
Belinda was not tough or old,
Or terrible of mien;
She wasn’t what is call’d a scold.
But patient and serene ;
Yet, really, it was doosid droll
To see them tremble when she srao'k.
So cokl her beam, so cold and pure,
So different to most;
A gushing spinster’s grin, I’m sure,
Is often “ warm as toast ”
Which saying none could reconcile
With dear Belinda’s solemn smile.
She’d walk into a drawing-room
’Midst sounds of joy and glee,
When suddenly an awful gloom
Would fall on all but me;
This daughter of the Sunny South
Wore such a smile about her mouth.
’Twas full of gentle, meek reproof
Commingled with surprise—
It seemed to say, “I hold aloof
From earthly vanities,
And look on merriment as vile. ”
Thus dumbly spake Belinda’s smile.
I was a true Kerristian then
And taught in Sunday-school,
Nor cared for giddy, bad young men
Who drink and play the fool;
I used to offer Heaven thanks
For having made Belinda Banks.
She seemed the very sort of wife
A teacher ought to choose
To sanctify his path in life
And keep him off the booze.
I was a “mug,” I must allow—
But, bless your heart! I’m wiser now.
So in the chilly month of May
I claimed her for my own,
And marvelled, in the good old way.
How man can live alone
When such an angel from above
Would comfort him and be his love.
Three months, or so, had flitted by.
Three months of holy bliss,
Before Belinda wondered why
I talked like that or this ;
And, somehow, in a little while
A change came o’er Belinda’s smile.
It grew like those of other girls,
Those skittish “ bits of jam ” :
She learned to blush and shake her curls,
And once she muttered “ dam “;
So far, indeed, she even got
As mentioning a place that’s hot.
I was a true Kerristian still.
It grieved me to remark
How oft she went with cousin Bill
For rambles after dark,
And was so very apt to be
Alone with him, instead of me.
His manner wasn’t grave and kind.
Check-trousers, too, he wore—
But yet Belinda’s heart inclined
To William more and more ;
Till one day, when I came from church,
I found she’d left me in the lurch.
Her conduct cut me like a knife,
It cut me to the heart.
And though Belinda’s still my wife
Of course w r e live apart.
A change, too, has come over me—
I’m quite a rorty cup of tea.
The faithless one (I’ve understood
From men who know her well)
Looks better now she’s not so good ;
Her ballet name is “ Belle ”
She’s on the stage, and mashes earls.
And smiles like all the other girls.
1 had a moral ip my mind
When sitting down to this,
But now ’tis finished off I find
The moral’s gone amiss—
In point of fact there wasn’t time
For putting reason in the rhyme.


At the Old Bailey (London) one day last
month a young man attired in a slouch hat with a
battered brim appeared on a charge of attempted
murder and unfinished suicide. It transpired in
evidence that he earned £1 a week and allowed
his mother 10s. of that amount. He lived himself
on the other 10s., and having bestowed his affec-
tions on a girl of 17 they looked forward to the
time when they would be united, and feed on dry
bread in an attic for the rest of their lives. The
young lady hadn’t a cent., and was unable to get
work, so he shared the 10s. with her. Also he
took her aged father and her sister and her
crippled brother out of the workhouse, and the
whole five existed on a precarious income which
allowed them two shillings apiece weekly. By
and bye, however, the prisoner’s brain deterio-
rated, probably because he and the four paupers
lived riotously on the half-sovereign and gave way
to gluttony and overfeeding; anyhow one night
the distracted lover began to see visions and
things in his attic, and he went out and cut his
girl’s throat and his own, and then threw himself
downstairs. They both recovered, and Judge
Grantham, after hearing the evidence, heaved a
sigh that blew the criminal’s hat right out into
the street. Then he admonished him in a pensive
tone upon the sinfulness of his ways, and hinted that
he was fast going to perdition. Also, he discharged
him, and told him not to do it again, and took up
a subscription of ten guineas, and having handed
over the coin he bade the recipient clear out and
get married on the proceeds—which was hideously
bad advice.

A Lesson from the Strike.
Editor Bulletin, —Sir, were anything needed to
emphasise a lesson which has been inculcated by
every strike that has taken place by labour-unions
since their inception, it is being abundantly fur-
nished at the present crisis. The lesson to which
I refer, and which requires so much iteration and
reiteration to properly impress itself, is this—the
greatest enemy to the working man is the working
man himself. .
The unionist war is really not so much against
capital as against non-union labour; for if the
latter were not procurable the employer and employe
would be brought face to face on fair terms ; and
it is safe to assume that strikes would be very few,
and very short-lived when they occurred, if capital
were deprived of that whip to use against labour
which is supplied by the class of toilers who
selfishly keep aloof from their fellow-workers, and
who are known as “non-unionists.” It is quite
clear that if the world’s workers are ever to be
elevated to a higher plane of social comfort than
they have hitherto enjoyed, it can only be achieved
by mutual and, as near as possible, universal
combination.
What would be the effect of such a combination?
Every man having labour of any description to
sell would be placed in a position to treat with a
purchaser, but the minimum wage the worker
would have to receive, as well as the maximum
number of hours he would have to toil, would be
fixed beyond mistake. The existence of such a
state of things would, I firmly believe, not only
immensely increase the comfort and happiness of
every human being on earth who receives pay for
services rendered, but would also add to the hap-
piness and in no way diminish the comfort and
luxuries enjoyed by those who pay for labour.
What would it matter to a railway or other con-
tractor had he to pay higher wages to those who
did his work so long as the price he received was
proportionately high? What would it matter to
the owner of a great coal-mine if the men who
delved for him were made better off, so long as he
could command a higher price for the commodity
his purchased labour produced ? What would it
matter to the farmer if he were bound to pay his
helps and servants a higher wage and give them
shorter hours, so long as he received for
the product of their labour a higher price !
to compensate him for his extra expendi-
ture ? And so on with every conceivable
branch of employment and labour. The effect
would be not only an increase in the comfort
and contentment of the masses all the world over,
but it would ensure a sense of security as well to
the few who are called capitalists. Avenues of
employment would be open in all directions,
which are now kept closed because of mutual dis-
trust. Petty tyrannies which are now occasionally
practised owing to the employment of non-union
labour would cease to exist, because the necessity
for them had ceased to exist. There is not the
least doubt that by far the greatest cause of
trouble between unionists and capitalists is non-
union labour, nor can it be denied that so long as
the latter class of labour is at the command of
capital, so long will the efficiency of unionism be
comparatively feeble and of small effect.
This being so, it behoves those who have the
noble end in view—the universal combination of
labour—to use every effort to induce all men who !
depend upon their labour to unite for purposes of
mutual elevation and self-protection. An argu-
ment against unionism that one continually hears
uttered in certain quarters is—Why should not a
man who is willing to work be allowed to work,
how and when he chooses ?
The reason that he should not be so allowed is
that no man has a right to do any deed or
commit any act calculated to injuriously
affect his fellow-men; and by endeavouring
to nullify the act of hundreds of union men
who have seen fit to strike as a means of
rectifying what they conceive to be a wrong, the
non-unionist strikes a heavy and forceful blow at
the emancipation of every human creature who
depends upon toil for existence.
If the capitalist could only be made to see that
universal unionism (which must, by the way, come
sooner or later) would not only not injure, but
really improve his position, and if the non unionist
could only be made to see the same, one of the
greatest problems the human race has to solve
would, I believe, have found a practical solution.
In conclusion, I cannot do better than repeat
that the greatest enemy to the working man is
the working man himself.
Yours, &o., W. G.
[Even should all the world’s workers become unionists,
the labour problem would not be solved by any means.
The monopolist would not then have the power to keep
down wages by playing the unemployed against the em-
ployed, but then, as now, it would be necessary to his
existence that he should limit production and therefore
limit the number of producers. With the monoply of
land, the source of wealth, and capital, the tool for its
production, still at his back, this limitation could easily be
maintained. Consequently, under universal unionism,
individual pay might be higher, but as the unionist in
work would be compelled to support the unionist out of
work, the aggregate earnings of labour would probably
not be increased and the advantage would be one of
little value. Nor, while production is for profit and not
tor use, can things be altered for the better.— Ed. B.)
The Melbourne Charity Organisation Secre-
tary admits that last year his Society spent £400
in salaries and £12 in relief.
Mappin, a veteran, gives information in
Melbourne about the first Yictorian gold discovery.
A shepherd brought a nugget, weighing 4 oz., to
Melbourne in 1849. It was shown to Superin-
tendent Latrobe, and a reward claimed, but
Latrobe would do nothing. The nugget was on
view for months in the window of the Waterman’s
Arms Hotel.
A Melbourne husband, cross-examining
the missus in Richmond police-court, asked her
whether she didn’t express a desire to see him run
over by a railway-engine. A flush of indignation
suffused the lady’s face. She had never men-
tioned an engine in connection with her partner
for life. Between sobs she admitted to having
casually remarked that dear Janies would be much
better at the bottom of the Yarra, and it was a
pity a horse didn’t kick his brains out.
“ Max O’Rell” asks : “Why are the bulk
of French marriages far happier than the bulk of
English ones ? In my opinion it is simply because,
according to the French system, a man has to win
the affection of his wife after the wedding. The
French married couple begin at the first act,
whereas the English one has generally got through
all but the fifth by the time the wedding-bells
have stopped ringing. Then in come the prosaic
cares of life and cover everything up. Out go the
lights, and, presto ! the glamour is gone, too, and
nothing remains of it all but a sleepy numbness
and a sort of wonder that the comedy should enjoy
such a world-wide reputation. Yes, in matrimony
there is a way of eating your cake and yet having
it; but you must not take your bites too large in
the beginning. As a clever wit says, ‘ When you
are dead, it is for a long time; but when you are
married, it is for ever.’”

The U.S. National Farmers’ Alliance and
Industrial Union is said to have more than two
million names on the membership roll. It has
70,000 secretaries. There is a similar organisation
composed of coloured men, with a million mem-
bers belonging to 30 States, White men are not
admitted into the alliance of the coloured men,
and coloured men may not join the white men’s
union; but the two bodies work in harmony.
Louise the forlorn, recently arrived late at a
concert given in her honour at the house of Mrs.
Mackay (the silver queen who never took in wash-
ing), just four hours behind time, in fact, as the
string quartette was putting the pianissimo touch
on someone’s “Op 50.” The Princess wasn’t a
bit disconcerted, she just got them to go through
the programme all over again (she is so fond of
music), and when they finished at 4 a.m., she was
the only one in the crowd who didn’t look sleepy
and wasn’t at heart in a vile temper.
Kicking is the latest and healthiest exercise
for ladies. It reduces any tendency to corpulence,
makes the limbs round and graceful, and adds
wonderfully to the vigour and elasticity of the
entire frame. Every lady should kick hard for an
hour before going to bed and for half an hour
immediately after rising in the morning. When
preparing for a ball it is advisable to kick with
desperate ferocity for about ten minutes in order
to get the muscles in good condition. Care re-
quires to be taken in the early stages of this
exercise, else the amateur may knock the furniture
into splinters, and drive the bedstead and wash-
stand through the window; but after a time she
grows active and expert, and then she can fly up
and hit a mosquito on the mantelpiece with both
feet, and return lightly to the floor without doing
the slightest damage to her surroundings.
A Yankee paper publishes the following
libellous and outrageous statement on the lack of
beauty in British Royalty and aristocracy :
What a fiction it is to talk about the beauty of any
member of the English Royal family. There could not be
a more commonplace lot of women. The Princess of
Wales is slender almost to emaciation ; her eyes would be
dull but for the make-up which surrounds them ; her
cheeks are rouged and a wig always covers her head,
which, lam given to understand, is completely bald. She
has little if any bust, she limps as she walks, and she is
almost stone deaf ; so, one way and another, it is rather
sickening to read the mass of gush that is to be read daily
in the British newspapers about the beauty, &c., of Eng-
land’s future Queen. She is, no doubt, an accomplished,
amiable, virtuous woman, but in appearance she is an
absolutely commonplace and altogether uninteresting
person. All three of the Princesses of Wales have in-
different teeth, bad complexions, and wretched figures.
Nobody would look at them twice but for their
exalted position. The daughters of Princess Christian
are equally plain looking, and there is no pro-
mise of beauty in the young daughters of the
Duchess of Edinburgh. As for Princess May of Teck,
who has so often been described as a beauty, all she can
claim to possess is a passable figure and good eyes. Her
face, however, is spoiled by the way in which her upper
lip protrudes and by a nose which suggests an Hebraic
strain somewhere or other in her family. Of the many
vaunted beauties of London society only the Duchess of
Leinster would, perhaps, pass as a lovely woman all over
the earth. Lady Londonderry also comes under that
head, and so does Miss Wolseley, the young daughter of
Lord Wolseley, who made her debut this year. Lady
Dudley is now completely passes, and is as much made
up as an opera bouffe prima donna. All the Duchesses
are plain-looking women; even the two American
Duchesses, Marlborough and Winchester, are too far
beyond their youth to claim affiliation with Venus. The
Duchess of Portland looks like a well-fed dairymaid, and
the Duchess of Newcastle bears a marked resemblance to
a younger edition of Mrs. Cleveland. The Dowager
Duchess of Montrose is a sight for the gods, with her
flaxen wig and her canary-coloured garments, not to men-
tion her enormous proportions. She reminds one of the
better-half of a suddenly-enriched German brewer from
Chicago. She certainly would not be permitted to enter
a fashionable New York hotel until her rank had been
explained. The Duchesses of Rutland, Leeds, Cleveland

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, October 1,1890.
Mt Dear Moorabinda. —
It is becoming noticeable at high-class concerts that
the ordinary encore—which is really an act of unmeaning
politeness on the part of an enduring audience—is not
accounted an encore, and that only artists desperately
eager to screech come on grasping a roll of music as they
wriggle their acknowledgments. “Once a recall—twice
an encore,” is the rule at present, and the genius who
comes back first time and springs another ditty on a
courteous crowd who really didn’t mean to ask for more,
is apt to pipe to a silent house during the rest of the pro-
gramme.
An irascible and verdant person “ writes to the papers ”
complaining of the habit “ girls and women” in Sydney
have of “ gaping into shop-windows to discover the
catchpenny when they could step inside and purchase
and not obstruct the thoroughfare.” But this vexed
individual doesn’t remember that shop-windows reflect as
well as display. It’s not the catchpenny nor even their
own “ full length ” these absorbed gown-gazers are after —
they are simply keeping an eye on the foot-passengers,
and when the favoured one approaches she turns so
innocently, “ Ah ! how do you do, Mr. FitzJonesf It’s
ever so much better than playing spider on the door-mat.
On dlt that a suburban medico, in conjunction with a
compounder of whatever goes to make up a threepenny
ice, has arranged to have these flabby refreshers passed
round the Sydney theatres in place of the bilious choco-
late-cream which now does the rounds of the circle. Ice-
creams ever tempt the young and thoughtless in the
summer season, and the innovation will probably catch
<w until somebody dies of peritonitis on the premises, or
until it significantly leaks out that the other member of
the firm is an undertaker.
The abandoned idea of a farewell ball to Lady Caring-
ton, who will accept neither bauble nor hall from her
hivin’ subjects generally, savoured very much of “one
for you and two for myself,” unless it was designed to
keep the dance impossibly private, For at all such affairs
Lady Carington dances two formal squares and perohe s
on the dais for the rest of the evening, while people
promenade slowly by, blinking furtively or openly glaring
at her, and representative ladies flounder uninvited into
her presence, while weird people, whom she cannot re*
member, bow impressively from all quarters until mid-
night, when she withdraws quietly and her part in the
festivity is over.
The greatest aggravation one has to endure at the
dressmaker’s is to occupy the same box with the bride-
eiect who has come to try on the wedding-gown—with
one sleeve out or the darts unsewn, to counteract the
supposed baleful influences which attend the fitting-on
of the completed robe of sacrifice. “ I shall be so nervous,”
she explains to the “buttoner” who inevitably accom-
panies her, “ but Harry won’t be.” “Do you think Harry
likes the bridesmaids' hats f” “ Oh, this train is really an
inch too much—Harry hates them long,” etc. This
deceives everybody who doesn’t know men wen enough
to be cynically sure that if Harry had been really studied
in the matter, she would have slipped into a registrar’s
in her quietest walking-gown, and tied the knot before an
official in shirt-sleeves.
There is division in Sydney war-circles—over a millinery
question of course. The absorbing question is whether
the Volunteers shall have gold lace on their uniforms,
and the officers to a unit are understood to say that
should their men be auriferously trimmed, the staff wil)
east aside their principal attraction and go forth on their
merits. With gold laoe frilled round his coat-tails, the
amateur Tommy Atkins would look like the result cf
drawing the wrong ticket in a raffle ; while to strip the
lace off the “ staff ” would be like robbing a soap-adver-
tisement of an eighteen-carat frame.
An enormous crowd of people, headed by Mayor and
Mrs. Burdekin, filled the yawning Centennial Hail, on
24th ult., to hear Sydney Liedertafel struggle through
“ Antigone,” set to Mendelssohn’s musie, with the aid of
a string orchestra and a confident “reader” of the
abridged dramatic portion, and though the effort of sing-
ing the difficult chant-like music with the words lopped
off in the most impossible places and slipping over into
bars where they have no business to be must be some-
thing like riding downstairs in a dray, still a knowledge
of the sufferings of the band of larks in white shirt-fronts
and kid gloves didn’t make one enjoy the classical out-
burst any the more. It was like going to church. The
reader would make an audible statement in an inoffensive
way, and the whole chorus would come down on him with
a bellow and criticise his mournful remarks from D below'
to upper A until he must have been sorry he spoke. The
second part of the programme introduced Mrs. Vander-
veer Greene in white,silk and crystal beads singing “ My
heart at thy sweet voice,” followed by Miss Baber in pale
primrose and tan gloves with “ Orpheus and his lute ”
giving Cowen’s “A World Between” as encore with a
magnificent disregard for words and tempo which almost
suggested that, the lady composed as she went along.
Kowalski—the other interpolated genius who lent variety
to the card—was encored for a “Grand Polonaise”
(Chopin), and scintillated through some fireworks on
“ The Last Rose of Summer ” to the joy of the back row,
who had no occasion to hold their ears. Mrs. Burdekin
wore black lacc designed in a floral stripe veiled over ecru
satih, elbow' sleeves of fulled gauze and tiny Medici collar
in black jet, a cluster of saffron roses on the bodice, and
the bouquet presented at the Manly Flower Show in white
actlnotus, epacris, ferns, &c. (gt
Sydney “Royal” Naval Home (an aquarium-like, wet-
plaster-colored, four-storied edifice), situated in Charlotte-
place was opened with much state on 25th inst. by Lord
Carington (accompanied by Lady Carington), in the
presence ot all the leading naval officers, headed by
Admiral Lord Charles Scott (who can’t even read a speech
without those breathless pauses and gaspings which one
only forgives in the orator relying solely on his wrist-
bands) and Admiral Valois, of the German warship now
in port, also a crowd of the public, who filled the dining-
room—the scene of the ceremony—to overflowing. The
house was a bower of westeria, roses, azaleas and camelias.
Lady Carington wore shot-pink silk and fawn velvet, and
moss roses in her bonnet; Lady Scott was in moss-green
woollen, relieved with black braid and moire. The cere,
monies were as usual. The Baron, who takes on another
tinge of saffron after each banquet, made a “ funny ”
speech, and scratched the back of every “ representative ”
person within a mile of the building, after which almost
everybody got a round of hoorays, including the kindly
Mr. Shearston, who has been appointed head of the house.
The other orators were Captain Dean and Admiral Scott.
The Naval Home is really a seaman’s residential
elub, and if only the management will curb its
evident desire for unlimited Moody and Sankey anti bo
economical in the length of its morning prayers, it ought
to be most popular with those who go down to the sea in
ships. The building (which cost £14,000) will accommodate
350 beds, and has smoking, reading, bath rooms, &c., and
several ladies intend holding concerts, amateur theatri-
cals and so on from time to time in its maritime shades.
Lord Carington made the Home a very handsome gift—a
large oil-painting of the “Battle of St. Vincent.”
The added attraction of two vocal numbers by a French
baritone of repute drew a big throng to the Hazou orches-
tral concert on 27th ult., when Lady Carington—in shot-
fawn and pink silk with fawn velvet sleeves, bonnet in
gold iaoe and blush roses, accompanied by Miss Walenn
in dark-blue tartan and feathered hat, and brother Rupert
in the sulks—accorded her patronage, for probably the
very last time, to the harmonious proceedings. Monsieur
Deslouis—the attraction—is a puffy, middle-aged baritone
with a larky eye and a winning little strut in his walk,
reminiscent of Handsome George in his kingliest
moments. He sang “ Les Rameaux” with orchestral
accompaniment, and modestly hung back until the encore
became clamorous, and later on Kowalski’s “ Gillies de
Bretagne ” (a woman behind me freely translated this as
“ AVaistooats of Britain !”), which allowed him to emit a
limit head-note with the piercing howl that always makes
a Sydney audience feel that it’s ten shillings haven’t fled
in vain, as the singer is a-burstin’ o’himself—of course
that was re-demanded. A double encore resulted, and it
was interesting to note how the kid No. 6's banged
together like cymbals. As a rule, female enthusiasm,
except for a Beliew, is limited to a few silly, noiseless pats
on the back of one hand, strikingly suggestive of a half-
hearted ’skeeter chase.
It is perfectly true that a stylish and comparatively
recent widow, engaged to gay December with an appal-
ing bank-account, takes the hoary bridegroom-expectant
with her to the frockist’s, where he is accommodated with
a front seat during the mystic rites of “ fitting-on.”
Madame Mantalini thinks it's “ papa,” of course, and the
billiard-ball-topped Romeo never contradicts a lady.
The all-pervading fashion of the beauty-spot veil gives
rise to the query, “AVhy do women always locate the
dot over the very worst feature in their face?" The
motive of the beauty-spot is, I presume, to draw atten-
tion to what you consider the speciality of your frontis-
piece. If you have a pretty mouth the spot is placed on
the corner ; nice eyes, on the cheekbone, and so on, but
few women seem to grasp this simple fact! I met a girl
recently with goose-eyes, and (I should think) most kissable
mouth—yet that guide-post spot was dangling over her
left eyebrow. Another fascinator had placed two spots
on either ear, as if her beauty lay there, which it didn’t,
while another had arranged the velvet semi-circle on the
confines of a mouth which turned down at the corners.
N-B. —Never marry a woman whose mouth turns down at
the corners.
The first re appearance of the gifted Best at the Cen-
tennial Hall, on the 37th ult., proved to a full house that
the talented gentleman still wears white weskits, and that
the largest organ in the world still sounds as if a number
of empty jam-tins had got mixed up loose inside when
the music is “maestoso,” while softer movements are so
tangled up with wind that they sound like a cow trying
to “moo” with a head cold. The Best recital served to
introduce the latest convert from comic-operaism, Marian
Burton, who has repudiated fleshings for the deml-train
of the conoert-platform, and who seemed all the time to
be repressing a fatal tendency to alternate each hand over
her heart while the other was curving gracefully in mid
air as was her wont as “Boooacoio” and other bifurcated
heroes. She wore an appealing costume in pale-pink silk
draped with crystal net, diamond ornaments and tan
gloves, and chose for her initial effort Hullah’s “ Three
Fishers,” a doleful ditty, with a leak in its raison d’etre,
since now-a-days the average woman has to weep and
work while the oki man goes on strike. There was no
dressing in front, and the women were mostly topped
with floral toques and carried reticules in their warm
right hands, while a blonde young man was the
“sinecure” of all eyes with a large black “beauty”
spot on his typical cheek.
It was played low down on Gov. Carington when ha
was invited to open the Sydney yachting season on 27th
inst. with the last banquet lying ominously high up and
a stiff nor’-easter blowing, Bill. Many a “ soft ” seaman,
of whom only two or three bias flannel seams were visible
over the stern, was understood to remark in a suffocating
way that he believed he could see coral growing at a great
depth. The day was glorious, with the sky blue as a
writ.
The second vice-regal ball on 25th ult. might have been
the first for all the difference there was. The two hops
were as alike as two peas, and most of the dresses had
been seen before, as is generally the case late in the
season. Westeria was the main idea in the decorations,
and if perhaps the perfume is very headachy in a close
room, still it looks handsome hanging from a nail; but
the knobby and obviously Republican waratah seemed
decorative matter out of place in the atmosphere of
Government House. Lady Carington received her guests
at the entrance to the red drawing-room and wore a pale
heliotrope silk with a soft front of chiffon and falling
sprays of westeria (about the most difficult flow er to make
a convincing artificial after, by the way), diamond orna-
ments, and bouquet of actonotus —her favourite Aus-
tralian flower—and opened the hall with Lord Charles
Scott, Lord Carington dancing with Lady Charles Scott,
dressed in white satin and chiffon well draped. There
was a good deal of colour on the floor and very few black
toilettes to detract from the sweetness and light of the
gathering, which was thoroughly enjoyed—especially by
the givers of the feast, who couldn’t help joyfully reflect-
ing, I know, that this was their very last social outbreak.
“ The New Magdalen ” is a bad title for the dry-sob
spectacle inaugurated at Her Majesty’s Theatre on 27th
ult. The play ought now to be called “ The Old
Magdalen,” for we have got so tired of her ! Life is too
short for play-actors to spontaneously ascend the stump
and give intoned disquisitions on subjects that were dead
fifty years ago; and then if one is cynically inclined, one
feels so keenly that Mercy Merrick would never have made
the restitution she did had Julian Grey not been looming
large on the premises, that the main thrill is only a sickly
spasm after all. As Mercy, Miss Achurch wears only
two nice gowns. No. A in crushed straw berry velveteen,
trimmed with silk beaded with passementerie to corres-
pond, No. 2 in brown velveteen with a Watteau book and
crinkled sleeves of flesh-pink silk, the Medici collar lined
with the silk.
The Manly Wild Flower Show (this year in aid of the
local Cottage Hospital), opened by Mayoress Burdekin,
was a big success, as such things go, and in no small
degree owed its attractiveness to the energy and good
taste displayed by Mrs. H. T. Robey, who superintended
the floral arrangements. Mr. H. T. Smith was awarded
first prize for the best floral design ; Miss Madeline Fitz-
Stubbs secured the prize for best bouquet; Miss Fox was
victorious in the best-dozen-buttonholes department;
and it was decreed that Miss Ella Richards had supplied
the best basket of flowers. A rather curious feature of
the show was that the appearance of Mrs. Burdekin was
greeted with the National Anthem.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
The Narrabri Oddfellows’ Ball took place on September
17, and we are informed by a local participant that a more
brilliant “ shivoo " never eventuated in that part of the
globe. Mr. A. T. Bather officiated as M. 0., and the
Misses Walsh and Coleman and Mesdames Goddard and
Lilieyman, in the writer’s opinion, earned the pastry.
The engagement of Captain Barlow, of H.M.S. Orlando,
to Miss Dight, daughter of Mr. Arthur Dight, of “ Lans-
downe,” Darling Point, Sydney, has been announced.
The captain of H.M.S. Royalist is also engaged—to marry
a rich Victorian widow.
The Buttercup spinsters held a successful dance at
Needs’ rooms on 26th ult., when the proceedings were
quite up to the title. Yellow was much worn, and posies
and button-holers of the glased yellow blossoms that have
had such a run lately were greatly affected. The affair
was most enjoyable.
A bevy of ladies intend to organise a “ Court ” ball, to
be held at West’s rooms at an early date, when powder
and patches will be the main idea for the ladies, while the
gentlemen will wear silk stockings and swords (obviously'
an invitation to amateur mounted policemen). Under
such circumstances it is pleasant to reflect that sawdust
is still down and sticking-plaster remains firm.
The plain and costume ball given by Mr. and Mrs.
George Brook, of “L’Avenue,” Newtown, was a most en-
joyable festivity, concluding in the early morning. Mrs.
Brock wore a very complete Dorothy dress; Miss Oarran,
pale heliotrope silk ; Miss E. Scott (Darling Point) peach-
coloured silk and cream lace ; Mrs. Fischer Cripps, black
silk and jet with blush roses. The music by a string
hand was excellent, and floor and supper were welt
managed.
The ball given by the gay bachelors of the Union Club,
last week, was a lively festivity, punctuated with pot
plants - the screening of Need’s band in a grove of scrip-
tural palms contributing greatly to the witchery of the
waltzes. Lady Carington wore white gauze striped with
silver with a garland of pale-pink roses, diamond tiara
necklet and bracelets—her posy of primroses and violets
hardly harmonising with the gown ; Lady Hopetoun in
biaok with a foot border of blush roses, diamond jewels;
Mrs. Aoland Hood also in black relieved with scarlet roses
and autumn leaves. Most of the dresses had made their
debut on a previous occasion.
Communications intended for this column should reach
The Bulletin office by Tuesday.
The theatrical profession, as well as the
amateurs, will learn with genuine satisfaction
that Mr. Gilbert J. Smith has opened a depdt
which he designates “Noah’s Ark” with a large
and a fully-assorted equipment of “ stage proper-
ties and requisites,” in Pitt-street, opposite the Cri-
terion, Sydney. Mr. Smith should have his hands
full, as he has no evident competition in his line in
Sydney.
The enterprising firm of the London and
Sydney Tea Company have removed their offices
to 167 Clarence-street. This company is going
straight ahead, acting up to its motto of a single
package at wholesale price, thus making every
cocky and farmer in the land his own tea-mer-
chant. Their travellers visit every homestead in
this colony and Queensland, and are made welcome
at the front-door by the missus, while the boss of
the shanty, armed with a flint-lock gun or a rusty
sword, chases the much-maljgned book-agent out
the back way, clear of the cultivation. Their
teas can with confidence be recommended, and
such recommendation will not be abused, as the
company pride themselves on the fact that their
ever-increasing business is due solely to their
principle of giving full value for money received.
Victorian Anglicans breathe freely now
that they have seen—and heard —the new Pri-
mate. There was always the chance that Right
Reverend “Saumarez Sydney” might intel-
lectually dwarf Field Flowers Melbourne. But
with Sydney’s appearance in Christ Church
(South Yarra) pulpit the other evening the
Victorians’ fear has vanished, and the smug com-
placency of the pious parties who are so grateful
for affliction (when it visits their neighbours) has
taken its place. The only point of comparison
between the two bishops is that it’s a toss-up
which is the more commonplace. The Primate’s
address at Christ Church was pitifully weak. With
the &ite of Melbourne Anglicism for his congrega-
tion, he got off some flabbiness which would have
discredited a Bible Christian local preacher at a
country circuit tea-fight. The only remark
worthy of notice was a miserable solecism.
Referring to the “ union of the Christian with the
Christian’s God,” he made his allusion by a paltry
attempt at emphasis with an antithesis which
reflected disadvantageously on Labour Unions.
There could be not the slightest doubt in the mind
of anyone who heard him that, when he con-
trasted the union of the “Church” with
“the unions which do not help but hinder,”
he was giving a back-hander to Trades-Unionism.
Which was a proceeding not at all surprising con-
sidering that just nine hours had elapsed since he
had landed in Melbourne, and almost every
moment of the time had been spent in monopolistic
company, but it was a most impolitic proceeding
all the same, if the Primate really intends to act
up to the statement expressed at Bishop Goe’s
garden-party —“ that his chief desire is to influence
the working-man.” The two bishops perambulated
Melbourne streets on Saturday last and many
Melbournites had to take a second look to make
sure which was Goe and which was the Primate.
This remark will at once identify the new-comer
as possessing a most imposing physique. The
Bulletin is not going, on so short an acquaint-
ance, to pronounce the new Primate a Chadband,
but he has got the Chadbandish trick of piling up
epithets. “ Dear Christian brethren and Christian
sisters and Christian friends —the union and the
co-operation and the joining together—the Chris-
tian mind, and the Christian heart, and the
Christian spirit ’’—are chunks from his Melbourne
address.
We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. .. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

The Australian Workman having asserted
that Italians “ bring nothing here but their fleas,
which are extra fat and voracious, and by them-
selves christened, for that reason, capuchins and
are disinclined for work, the Italians of Sydney
met in solemn conclave and decided that,
“proud of the importance and glory of
the Italian name, they think it deroga-
tory to their dignity to reply to such attacks.”
These onslaughts upon cheap labour should be
more discriminate. Labour has no reason to fear
any worker who lands here with his hands un-
trammelled. It is the man bound by previously-
signed contract to toil for old country wages that
becomes a dangerous foe. It is necessary to shut
out the cheap-living Italian, German, Britisher,
who, like rice-eating Chinamen, are to all intents
the slave of the middleman who import them, but
the intelligent, industrious freeman of any country
is a valuable ally.
Ask for Wolfe’s Schnapps and take no other.
The press-organ of Mr. Willis, M-F-j
opprobriously refers to Mr. Bruce Smith as “ a
Jew.” If the N.S.W. Minister for Works has in
his veins any Jewish blood he should be by n»
means ashamed of the fact; whereas Mr. Willis.
who seeks to strengthen a bad case by an appeal
to racial prejudice, ought to be absolutely ashamed
of himself. Mr. Willis boasts of being »
Christian man,” but we take the liberty of doubt-
ing if he solidifies his alleged position as such by
sneering at the race of the Saviour who he behevee
died between two thieves to save him. The Jew,
trampled for long ages beneath the feet of tyranny,
may, now that he has got into freedom’s sun-
light, often be unduly aggressive ; in that respect
he is, from similar causes, on equal terms with the
peasant-millionaire who has arisen from the Irish
bogs and made a fortune in a new and freer
country. To the Jews the world owes much
beside its Saviour. -tyWhere is the race which can
vie with the Hebrew people in the glory or its
muster-roll of brilliant men and women in the
domains of letters, arts and song ?

CADBURY’S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds
supplying a refined thin infusion ol absolutely pure
Cocoa, exhilarating and refreshing, for Breakfast, Lun-
cheon, Tea or Supper, giving staying power and im-
parting new life and vigour to growing children, and
those of delicate constitutions.
Tales of “The System.”
No.XII.— “The Hanging of the Eighteen’
(Knotting the Rope).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Rev. Thomas Taylor, 8.A., Pro-
testant Chaplain of the Penal Establishment,
Norfolk Island, had some curious notions. One
of them was to keep posted over the entrance of
his study a sheet of paper on which be had
neatly printed with his pen these words ;
“ Before tub Throne of Zeus,” says Hesiod, “ Dike
WEEVB WHENEVER THE EARTHLY JUDGE DECIDES WRONGLY.”
Underneath this quotation he had written in
his ordinary handwriting this sentence;
“No wonder, then, sculptors represent Justice with
hound eyes. She has gone weeping blind.”
Below this, again, he had jotted down a long
aeries of dates. Against each date he had put a
word —generally a man’s name—to serve as a
memory-peg. It was thus ho kept a succinct
record of the more unjust sentences pronounced
on the Island by Summary or Superior Court
the Saturday evening on which the Special
Commission concluded its sittings, Mr. Taylor,
as soon as the prisoners were removed, went
straight from the Temporary Court House, alias
the Butcher's Shop, to his study.
Hearing his footstep on the verandah, where the
moonlight was playing hide-and-seek with the
shadows east by the trellis, Mrs. Taylor, who was
waiting for him in her sitting-room, called out:
“Tea is ready, dear !”
“i’ll be with you presently,’ he replied. i
am simply completing my list.” As he laid down
his pen, after inscribing an addition to the paper
attached to the wall, he murmured to himself: ‘ I
may draw a line now. To-day is the climax.
Justice will weep no more after to-day, for the
fount of her tears will have dried up.”
And he took his ruler and a pencil, and traced
a thick stroke under his final entry, which read
14th November, 184 —. Special Commission (Qay-
thorpe). Arraignments, 57 ; acquittals, 9. Convictions
Death, 20 ; death recorded, 3 ; life, 7 ; ten years, 5 ; four
years, 12. Tappin’s case was reserved for the Chief
Justice.
The parson underscored Tappin’s name—doubly
underscored it, as though otherwise he might
forget the special fact which it was his wish to
remember. He went into the pleasant room
where his wife was waiting for him. As she
turned up the lamp she watched his face. Through
its pallor, like that of the moonlight, glared a,
great pain, and when he bent his hands into his
palms, as he craved a blessing on the food, he
let them rest there so long that she grew alarmed.
“Are you ill, dear?” she asked, at last.
“No,” he replied, looking up, “not ill, but
pained—wearied —puzzled—puzzled that such
things are permitted to be. Twenty—great God !
Twenty /—are to hang on Monday morning at
nine o’clock!” ... .. .
He glanced at his watch and then continued
“In 35 hours and 20 minutes 20 men hang—
hang — hang ! And I am their pastor—their spiri-
tual father, responsible for them to God !;’ _
He placed his head on the table and his misery
wrung a moan from him.
Mrs. Taylor was a good woman and anything
but a commonplace woman, and her husband’s
tenderness had stood between her and the petrify-
ing influences of the System. Still there was
something of the conventional in her nature, and
it must have been this element that prompted her
next observation. Your good woman is so fearful
of new paths that for anything to have been done
before is to confer a kind of sanctity both on the
deed and its doer. Stifling her own sensation of
horror at his news, she tried to relieve him of part
of his burden,
“ I can’t understand, dear,” she said, “why you
should be so much affected. Many clergymen
have had similar responsibilities .”
“ The Law and the System took care of that!”
bitterly interjected Mr. Taylor.
“ And they did not break down,” his wife went
on. “You know what you often say in the
pulpit—‘ The back is strengthened to the burden. ’ ”
“Yes, yes! ” he answered her, with a sad im-
patience. “So it is; and I’ll get through the
work somehow —to the satisfaction of Mr. Scragge,
at least. But it’s not myself I’m thinking of, God
knows ! It’s the men—the twenty —to think of
them going before their Maker and their Judge as
they are—sin-stained, crime-stained, hardened of
heart, reprobate of conscience—blaspheming the
Deity, cursing their fellow-men, or jesting and
jeering with gibes on their lips that are viler than
curses —oh, ’tis terrible !”
His wife had never seen him so moved. She
tried to soothe him with the little tricks of manner
and speech that womanhood calls to its aid when
it wishes to calm the irritation of the menkind.
She rose, and passed her arm round his neck.
“Dear,” she said softly, “don’t take it so much
to heart! It’s not even as though there was no
one to help you in preparing these poor men for—
for Monday. There is Father MTndoe, you know,
and Mr. Ford ”
The Rev. Mr. Taylor, not being altogether a
saint, had more than once during their wedded
existence answered his wife rather rudely, but he
had never acted roughly towards her, or used in
her presence coarse language.
Judge, therefore, of her surprise when he,
rising, flung off her arm, and not so much said as
shouted ;
“Damn Mr. Ford!”
* # •
Seeing his wife’s astonishment and sorrow
very plainly written on her features, the Chaplain
stopped in the walk in which he was swinging
himself up and down the room.
“ I am very sorry, Mary, that I forgot myself.
I beg your pardon—but my nerves are unstrung
strange isn’t it” (here he laughed hysterically)
“that I’m unstrung because they’re to be up-
strung.” , ,
“ Tom! ” said his wife, greatly pained and
alarmed. “Tom !”
“ Yes, dear, I’m wrong I know—l shouldn t
pun on such a thing, should I ? But I’m going to
turn over a new leaf—l’m not going to feel anymore
—l’m going to harden —I’m going to be a faithful
and subservient servant to the System, like —like
my senior, Ford. ’
“ Tom!” once more reproachfully exclaimed Mrs.
Taylor.
“Oh, yes, I am, Mary. I shall model mysell
in future on Brother Ford ; good man, Brother
Ford —most useful man, Brother Ford. Is there
a juror short? Brother Ford will act with plea-
sure! Does the Attorney-General stumble for
want of a precedent ? Brother Ford will furnish
one! Is the Commissioner in doubt as to the
law? Brother Ford, from the jury-box, settles
the point, with the assurance of a Lord Chief
Justice ! Does the Olcrk of Arraigns bungle in
mi indictment? Brother Ford is at his elbow
with a correction ! Is a prisoner’s record faulty .
Brother Ford supplies a material omission from
the treasures of his memory—says the prisoner at
the bar killed a woman in fiobart Town nine
years ago—record says nothing—Brother lord
asks from the jury-box, prisoner at the bar, didn t
he? —prisoner at the bar replies yes! Oh, most
useful man, Ford ! He’ll assist me with the last
Communion of the Condemned —and then, per-
haps, offer to assist the hangman —perhaps be one
of the hangmen, for they’ll want two on Monday,
will Brother Ford !”
“Tom! are you mad ?”
“ No, dear—not mad ; at least I don’t think so.
Parson Ford isn’t mad, and the System adores
him, and I’m going to follow his example. 1 will
get another hundred pounds a year when I m like
him —‘ increment of salary to the Rev. 1 nomas
Taylor, 8.A., for hardening his heart. One
hundred pounds! that would read well in the
annual report, wouldn’t it, dear ? Ah ! ah ! Iso,
I’m not mad —I have been—l’m getting sane
now. I shall not fight the System any longer;
I’ll knock under, as the —men —say. 1 he System s
beaten me.” . .
Whether it was the System, or physical weak-
ness, that had defeated Mr. Taylor, we can’t say ;
but ho had no sooner spoken the last words than
he fell to the floor—not merely beaten, indeed, but
completely “knocked out.” His utterance had
been getting thicker and less intelligible to his
wife’s shocked ears, and as his speech grew more
incoherent, his walk changed from a slide to a
stagger.
Half-an-hour subsequently Dr. Grocott pro-
nounced him to be suffering from bram-fever.
During the preceding week the Chaplain had been
subjected to an intense physical and mental strain
by the tragedy in the chapel and then by the pro-
ceedings of the Special Commission. The awful
scenes which had characterised the session, with
their ghastly prologue, had had the effect of
breaking away the link which held an already
over-taxed constitution to health and sanity.
To this circumstance it was that the Condemned,
or, rather, such of them as were Protestants, were
indebted for that most precious of privileges—the
attendance of Senior Chaplain the Rev. iheophilus
Ford on them during their last hours.
* * *
It was something, let us tell you, for a
“ Black Norfolker ” to have Parson Ford’s minis-
trations. It was a distinction by virtue of rarity,
for the rare is, or should be, always precious. Not
that the Senior Chaplain’s attendance at these little
affairs of ceremony was a rare event —not at all;
for, up to the year of which we write. Parson
Ford had been present at upwards of
“ swingings,” as he termed them. Like his great
namesake and distant connection, the celebrated
Ordinary of Newgate, he enjoyed within his own
sphere a monopoly of the gallows. That sphere,
however, was at Hobart Town and its neighbour-
ing convict-stations, and so constantly pressing
were his duties there that he had never previously
spared the time for a trip to the Island. To tell
the truth, the Senior Chaplain could never absent
himself from his accustomed corner of the Vine-
yard without coming under the influence of an
uneasy feeling that things would go wrong in the
Capital. It was as though he suspected that a
criminal, who had the misfortune to be hanged
while he was away would receive but a cold wel-
come from the door-guard of Hades. Fortunately,
however, for his health’s sake and the equanimity
of his tamper, the consideration suggested it sell
that when visiting the Island the Los® °t the
Condemned in Hobart Town would be the Lam
of the Condemned at Norfolk; The latter would
have him if the former had him not. And so it
was that when Chaplain Taylor broke down, his
senior was ready at a moments notice to take his
place.
• # «
He preached to the Condemned on the
Sunday morning. Fifteen of them listened to
him ; the other five doomed to die were Roman
Catholics, and were at that moment in sobbing
penitence before Father M‘lndoe. It was very
impressive, that discourse, and on the whole in
very good taste —for Parson Ford, that is. The
chief fault that such of his auditors as were
principally concerned with its application found
in it was that it had not been specially written for
the occasion. . .
As they stood on the Chapel corridor waiting
for the word to move off to the condemned cells,
whence the majority of them, perhaps all (for it
was not yet known whether any would be re-
prieved) would never emerge except on to the
gallows, they discussed the discourse.
“ Well,” said Gooch, “ as th’ ol’ man ain’t 6d so
big a job as this afore, I did think he’d hey taken
some trouble over it, but I’m danged if it aimt th
same ol’ stuff as he shelled out the day afore I was
’preeved (reprieved) in ’38.”
“Yes,” said Martin Cash, “I remember. Bush-
ranger Cash was wearing his 471 b. irons that day ;
his Sunday suit, as he called them, for they were
never put on him except when he was taken to
chapel. His ordinary irons scaled at forty pounds,
but, doubtless to aid his devotions, an additional
half-stone was locked on him on chapel-days.
(You can see the Sunday-suit in Oscott Museum,
near Birmingham, England, any day you may
happen to bo in the neighbourhood. Bishop
Willson took them home to show a committee of
the House of Commons—meddlesome fellow, that
Bishop.) “ I remember,” repeated Cash; “ I was
one of the crew that time, warn’t I, Goochy?”
“ Yer was, Cash, lad,” said Gooch. “They
'preeved you same time as me. ”
“ Yes,” rejoined the once mighty ’ranger, medi-
tatively ; “yes, they 'preeved me—l never ’ad no
luck at all.' I ’orter been worked oi’f years an’
years ago. Three times ordered to be scragged
an’ never scragged onst! Drew the straw onst,
too, an’ th’ sneak funked.* Never ’ad no luck
since I stuck up thet’ooman at Cons’tushun ill
an’ rob’d ’er of ’er fawney.t Boys’—here bespoke
with a mock earnestness, as though he were some
venerable preacher, beseeching innocent young
souls not to wander from the paths they should
go—“ Boys, as sure as ye rob a’ooman ye’ll’ave
bad luck, and ’tickerly if you rob ’er of ’er fawney.
Don’ ye never, boys—else ye’ll ’ave bad luck.”
They mimicked his solemnity as they severally
assured him “by their honor,” “by their ’davy
(affidavit)” and by sundry other standards of
asseveration that they would never—no never-do
such a thing. And as all Martin’s auditors were
* “ Drew the straw.”—Three men, tired of life, would
get together, and, by drawing straws of various lengths,
decide which of the three was to murder one of the
others, and thus secure “liberty” for the lot—for the
murderer and the witness would be hanged. The victim
was said to “ draw the straw.” Occasionally, hut rarely,
the man who drew the longest straw, and was thereby
selected as the murderer, “funked” the awful task. The
straw-drawing ceremony is said to have occurred nearly
forty times at Norfolk Island.
